Arnold Sherman thought of Theodora 's ripe beauty , and the mellow companionableness she have reveal in their brief intercourse .
`` I 'm not perfectly sure of that , '' he say , with a half sigh .
II .
Old Lady Lloyd I .
The May Chapter Spencervale gossip always say that `` Old Lady Lloyd '' be rich and mean and proud .
Gossip , a usual , be one-third right and two-thirds wrong .
Old Lady Lloyd be neither rich nor mean ; in reality she be pitifully poor -- so poor that `` Crooked Jack '' Spencer , who dig her garden and chop her wood for her , be opulent by contrast , for he , at least , never lack three meal a day , and the Old Lady could sometimes achieve no more than one .
But she WAS very proud -- so proud that she would have die rather than let the Spencervale people , among whom she have queen it in her youth , suspect how poor she be and to what strait be sometimes reduce .
She much prefer to have them think her miserly and odd -- a queer old recluse who never go anywhere , even to church , and who pay the small subscription to the minister 's salary of anyone in the congregation .
`` And her just rolling in wealth ! ''
they say indignantly .
`` Well , she do n't get her miserly way from her parent .
THEY be real generous and neighbourly .
There never be a finer gentleman than old Doctor Lloyd .
He be always do kindness to everybody ; and he have a way of do them that make you feel a if you be do the favour , not him .
Well , well , let Old Lady Lloyd keep herself and her money to herself if she want to .
If she do n't want our company , she do n't have to suffer it , that 's all .
Reckon she be n't none too happy for all her money and pride . ''
No , the Old Lady be none too happy , that be unfortunately true .
It be not easy to be happy when your life be eat up with loneliness and emptiness on the spiritual side , and when , on the material side , all you have between you and starvation be the little money your hen bring you in .
The Old Lady live `` away back at the old Lloyd place , '' a it be always call .
It be a quaint , low-eaved house , with big chimney and square window and with spruce grow thickly all around it .
The Old Lady live there all alone and there be week at a time when she never saw a human be except Crooked Jack .
What the Old Lady do with herself and how she put in her time be a puzzle the Spencervale people could not solve .
The child believe she amuse herself count the gold in the big black box under her bed .
Spencervale child hold the Old Lady in mortal terror ; some of them -- the `` Spencer Road '' fry -- believe she be a witch ; all of them would run if , when wander about the wood in search of berry or spruce gum , they saw at a distance the spare , upright form of the Old Lady , gather stick for her fire .
Mary Moore be the only one who be quite sure she be not a witch .
`` Witches be always ugly , '' she say decisively , `` and Old Lady Lloyd be n't ugly .
She 's real pretty -- she 's get such a soft white hair and big black eye and a little white face .
Those Road child do n't know what they 're talk of .
Mother say they 're a very ignorant crowd . ''
`` Well , she do n't ever go to church , and she mutter and talk to herself all the time she 's pick up stick , '' maintain Jimmy Kimball stoutly .
The Old Lady talk to herself because she be really very fond of company and conversation .
To be sure , when you have talk to nobody but yourself for nearly twenty year , it be apt to grow somewhat monotonous ; and there be time when the Old Lady would have sacrifice everything but her pride for a little human companionship .
At such time she felt very bitter and resentful toward Fate for have take everything from her .
She have nothing to love , and that be about a unwholesome a condition a be possible to anyone .
It be always hard in the spring .
Once upon a time the Old Lady -- when she have not be the Old Lady , but pretty , wilful , high-spirited Margaret Lloyd -- have love spring ; now she hat them because they hurt her ; and this particular spring of this particular May chapter hurt her more than any that have go before .
The Old Lady felt a if she could NOT endure the ache of it .
Everything hurt her -- the new green tip on the fir , the fairy mist down in the little beech hollow below the house , the fresh smell of the red earth Crooked Jack spade up in her garden .
The Old Lady lay awake all one moonlit night and cry for very heartache .
She even forget her body hunger in her soul hunger ; and the Old Lady have be hungry , more or less , all that week .
She be live on store biscuit and water , so that she might be able to pay Crooked Jack for dig her garden .
When the pale , lovely dawn-colour come steal up the sky behind the spruce , the Old Lady bury her face in her pillow and refuse to look at it .
`` I hate the new day , '' she say rebelliously .
`` It will be just like all the other hard , common day .
I do n't want to get up and live it .
And , oh , to think that long ago I reach out my hand joyfully to every new day , a to a friend who be bring me good tidings !
I love the morning then -- sunny or gray , they be as delightful a an unread book -- and now I hate them -- hate them -- hate them ! ''
But the Old Lady get up nevertheless , for she know Crooked Jack would be come early to finish the garden .
She arrange her beautiful , thick , white hair very carefully , and put on her purple silk dress with the little gold spot in it .
The Old Lady always wear silk from motif of economy .
It be much cheap to wear a silk dress that have belong to her mother than to buy new print at the store .
The Old Lady have plenty of silk dress which have belong to her mother .
She wear them morning , noon , and night , and Spencervale people consider it an additional evidence of her pride .
As for the fashion of them , it be , of course , just because she be too mean to have them make over .
They do not dream that the Old Lady never put on one of the silk dress without agonize over it unfashionableness , and that even the eye of Crooked Jack cast on her antique flounce and overskirt be almost more than her feminine vanity could endure .
In spite of the fact that the Old Lady have not welcome the new day , it beauty charm her when she go out for a walk after her dinner -- or , rather , after her mid-day biscuit .
It be so fresh , so sweet , so virgin ; and the spruce wood around the old Lloyd place be athrill with busy spring doings and all sprinkle through with young light and shadow .
Some of their delight find it way into the Old Lady 's bitter heart a she wander through them , and when she come out at the little plank bridge over the brook down under the beech , she felt almost gentle and tender once more .
There be one big beech there , in particular , which the Old Lady love for reason best know to herself -- a great , tall beech with a trunk like the shaft of a gray marble column and a leafy spread of branch over the still , golden-brown pool make beneath it by the brook .
It have be a young sapling in the day that be haloed by the vanished glory of the Old Lady 's life .
The Old Lady hear childish voice and laughter afar up the lane which lead to William Spencer 's place just above the wood .
William Spencer 's front lane run out to the main road in a different direction , but this `` back lane '' furnish a short cut and his child always go to school that way .
The Old Lady shrink hastily back behind a clump of young spruce .
She do not like the Spencer child because they always seem so afraid of her .
Through the spruce screen she could see them come gaily down the lane -- the two old one in front , the twin behind , cling to the hand of a tall , slim , young girl -- the new music teacher , probably .
The Old Lady have hear from the egg pedlar that she be go to board at William Spencer 's , but she have not hear her name .
She look at her with some curiosity a they draw near -- and then , all at once , the Old Lady 's heart give a great bound and begin to beat a it have not beat for year , while her breath come quickly and she tremble violently .
Who -- WHO could this girl be ?
Under the new music teacher 's straw hat be mass of fine chestnut hair of the very shade and wave that the Old Lady remember on another head in vanished year ; from under those wave look large , violet-blue eye with very black lash and brow -- and the Old Lady know those eye as well a she know her own ; and the new music teacher 's face , with all it beauty of delicate outline and dainty colouring and glad , buoyant youth , be a face from the Old Lady 's past -- a perfect resemblance in every respect save one ; the face which the Old Lady remember have be weak , with all it charm ; but this girl 's face possess a fine , dominant strength compact of sweetness and womanliness .
As she pass by the Old Lady 's hiding place she laugh at something one of the child say ; and oh , but the Old Lady know that laughter well .
She have hear it before under that very beech tree .
She watch them until they disappear over the wooded hill beyond the bridge ; and then she go back home a if she walk in a dream .
Crooked Jack be delve vigorously in the garden ; ordinarily the Old Lady do not talk much with Crooked Jack , for she dislike his weakness for gossip ; but now she go into the garden , a stately old figure in her purple , gold-spotted silk , with the sunshine gleam on her white hair .
Crooked Jack have see her go out and have remark to himself that the Old Lady be lose ground ; she be pale and peaked-looking .
He now conclude that he have be mistake .
The Old Lady 's cheek be pink and her eye shin .
Somewhere in her walk she have shed ten year at least .
Crooked Jack lean on his spade and decide that there be n't many finer look woman anywhere than Old Lady Lloyd .
Pity she be such an old miser !
`` Mr. Spencer , '' say the Old Lady graciously -- she always speak very graciously to her inferior when she talk to them at all -- `` can you tell me the name of the new music teacher who be board at Mr. William Spencer 's ? ''
`` Sylvia Gray , '' say Crooked Jack .
The Old Lady 's heart give another great bound .
But she have know it -- she have know that girl with Leslie Gray 's hair and eye and laugh must be Leslie Gray 's daughter .
Crooked Jack spat on his hand and resume his work , but his tongue go fast than his spade , and the Old Lady listen greedily .
For the first time she enjoy and bless Crooked Jack 's garrulity and gossip .
Every word he utter be a an apple of gold in a picture of silver to her .
He have be work at William Spencer 's the day the new music teacher have come , and what Crooked Jack could n't find out about any person in one whole day -- at least as far a outward life go -- be hardly worth find out .
Next to discover thing do he love tell them , and it would be hard to say which enjoy that ensue half-hour more -- Crooked Jack or the Old Lady .
Crooked Jack 's account , boil down , amount to this ; both Miss Gray 's parent have die when she be a baby , she have be bring up by an aunt , she be very poor and very ambitious .
`` Wants a moosical eddication , '' finish up Crooked Jack , `` and , by jingo , she orter have it , for anything like the voice of her I never heerd .
She sing for u that even after supper and I think 't be an angel singing .
It just go through me like a shaft o ' light .
The Spencer young one be crazy over her already .
She 's get twenty pupil around here and in Grafton and Avonlea . ''
When the Old Lady have find out everything Crooked Jack could tell her , she go into the house and sit down by the window of her little sitting-room to think it all over .
She be tingle from head to foot with excitement .
Leslie 's daughter !
This Old Lady have have her romance once .
Long ago -- forty year ago -- she have be engage to Leslie Gray , a young college student who teach in Spencervale for the summer term one year -- the golden summer of Margaret Lloyd 's life .
Leslie have be a shy , dreamy , handsome fellow with literary ambition , which , a he and Margaret both firmly believe , would one day bring him fame and fortune .
Then there have be a foolish , bitter quarrel at the end of that golden summer .
Leslie have go away in anger , afterwards he have write , but Margaret Lloyd , still in the grasp of her pride and resentment , have send a harsh answer .
No more letter come ; Leslie Gray never return ; and one day Margaret waken to the realization that she have put love out of her life for ever .
She know it would never be hers again ; and from that moment her foot be turn from youth to walk down the valley of shadow to a lonely , eccentric age .
Many year later she hear of Leslie 's marriage ; then come news of his death , after a life that have not fulfil his dream for him .
Nothing more she have hear or know -- nothing to this day , when she have see his daughter pas her by unseeing in the beech hollow .
`` His daughter !
And she might have be MY daughter , '' murmur the Old Lady .
`` Oh , if I could only know her and love her -- and perhaps win her love in return !
But I can not .
I could not have Leslie Gray 's daughter know how poor I be -- how low I have be bring .
I could not bear that .
And to think she be live so near me , the darling -- just up the lane and over the hill .
I can see her go by every day -- I can have that dear pleasure , at least .
But oh , if I could only do something for her -- give her some little pleasure !
It would be such a delight . ''
When the Old Lady happen to go into her spare room that evening , she saw from it a light shin through a gap in the tree on the hill .
She know that it shine from the Spencers ' spare room .
So it be Sylvia 's light .
The Old Lady stand in the darkness and watch it until it go out -- watch it with a great sweetness breathing in her heart , such a risen from old rose-leaves when they be stir .
She fancy Sylvia move about her room , brushing and braid her long , glisten hair -- lay aside her little trinket and girlish adornment -- make her simple preparation for sleep .
When the light go out the Old Lady picture a slight white figure kneeling by the window in the soft starshine , and the Old Lady kneel down then and there and say her own prayer in fellowship .
She say the simple form of word she have always use ; but a new spirit seem to inspire them ; and she finish with a new petition -- `` Let me think of something I can do for her , dear Father -- some little , little thing that I can do for her . ''
The Old Lady have sleep in the same room all her life -- the one look north into the spruce -- and love it ; but the next day she move into the spare room without a regret .
It be to be her room after this ; she must be where she could see Sylvia 's light , she put the bed where she could lie in it and look at that earth star which have suddenly shine across the twilight shadow of her heart .
She felt very happy , she have not felt happy for many year ; but now a strange , new , dream-like interest , remote from the harsh reality of her existence , but none the less comforting and alluring , have enter into her life .
Besides , she have think of something she could do for Sylvia -- `` a little , little thing '' that might give her pleasure .
Spencervale people be wont to say regretfully that there be no Mayflowers in Spencervale ; the Spencervale young fry , when they want Mayflowers , think they have to go over to the barren at Avonlea , six mile away , for them .
Old Lady Lloyd know well .
In her many long , solitary ramble , she have discover a little clearing far back in the wood -- a southward-sloping , sandy hill on a tract of woodland belonging to a man who live in town -- which in spring be star over with the pink and white of arbutus .
To this clear the Old Lady betook herself that afternoon , walk through wood lane and under dim spruce arch like a woman with a glad purpose .
All at once the spring be dear and beautiful to her once more ; for love have enter again into her heart , and her starved soul be feast on it divine nourishment .
Old Lady Lloyd find a wealth of Mayflowers on the sandy hill .
She fill her basket with them , gloat over the loveliness which be to give pleasure to Sylvia .
When she get home she write on a slip of paper , `` For Sylvia . ''
It be not likely anyone in Spencervale would know her handwriting , but , to make sure , she disguise it , write in round , big letter like a child 's .
She carry her Mayflowers down to the hollow and heap them in a recess between the big root of the old beech , with the little note thrust through a stem on top .
Then the Old Lady deliberately hide behind the spruce clump .
She have put on her dark green silk on purpose for hiding .
She have not long to wait .
Soon Sylvia Gray come down the hill with Mattie Spencer .
When she reach the bridge she saw the Mayflowers and give an exclamation of delight .
Then she saw her name and her expression change to wonder .
The Old Lady , peer through the bough , could have laugh for very pleasure over the success of her little plot .
`` For me ! ''
say Sylvia , lift the flower .
`` CAN they really be for me , Mattie ?
Who could have leave them here ? ''
Mattie giggle .
`` I believe it be Chris Stewart , '' she say .
`` I know he be over at Avonlea last night .
And ma say he 's take a notion to you -- she know by the way he look at you when you be sing night before last .
It would be just like him to do something queer like this -- he 's such a shy fellow with the girl . ''
Sylvia frown a little .
She do not like Mattie 's expression , but she do like Mayflowers , and she do not dislike Chris Stewart , who have seem to her merely a nice , modest , country boy .
She lift the flower and bury her face in them .
`` Anyway , I 'm much oblige to the giver , whoever he or she be , '' she say merrily .
`` There 's nothing I love like Mayflowers .
Oh , how sweet they be ! ''
When they have pass the Old Lady emerge from her lurking place , flush with triumph .
It do not vex her that Sylvia should think Chris Stewart have give her the flower ; nay , it be all the good , since she would be the less likely to suspect the real donor .
The main thing be that Sylvia should have the delight of them .
That quite satisfy the Old Lady , who go back to her lonely house with the cockle of her heart all in a glow .
It soon be a matter of gossip in Spencervale that Chris Stewart be leave Mayflowers at the beech hollow for the music teacher every other day .
Chris himself deny it , but he be not believe .
Firstly , there be no Mayflowers in Spencervale ; secondly , Chris have to go to Carmody every other day to haul milk to the butter factory , and Mayflowers grow in Carmody , and , thirdly , the Stewarts always have a romantic streak in them .
Was not that enough circumstantial evidence for anybody ?
As for Sylvia , she do not mind if Chris have a boyish admiration for her and express it thus delicately .
She think it very nice of him , indeed , when he do not vex her with any other advance , and she be quite content to enjoy his Mayflowers .
Old Lady Lloyd hear all the gossip about it from the egg pedlar , and listen to him with laughter glimmer far down in her eye .
The egg pedlar go away and vow he 'd never see the Old Lady so spry a she be this spring ; she seem real interested in the young folk 's doings .
The Old Lady keep her secret and grow young in it .
She walk back to the Mayflower hill as long a the Mayflowers last ; and she always hide in the spruce to see Sylvia Gray go by .
Every day she love her more , and yearn after her more deeply .
All the long repressed tenderness of her nature overflow to this girl who be unconscious of it .
She be proud of Sylvia 's grace and beauty , and sweetness of voice and laughter .
She begin to like the Spencer child because they worship Sylvia ; she envy Mrs. Spencer because the latter could minister to Sylvia 's need .
Even the egg pedlar seem a delightful person because he bring news of Sylvia -- her social popularity , her professional success , the love and admiration she have win already .
The Old Lady never dream of reveal herself to Sylvia .
That , in her poverty , be not to be think of for a moment .
It would have be very sweet to know her -- sweet to have her come to the old house -- sweet to talk to her -- to enter into her life .
But it might not be .
The Old Lady 's pride be still far strong than her love .
It be the one thing she have never sacrifice and never -- so she believe -- could sacrifice .
II .
The June Chapter There be no Mayflowers in June ; but now the Old Lady 's garden be full of blossom and every morning Sylvia find a bouquet of them by the beech -- the perfumed ivory of white narcissus , the flame of tulip , the fairy branch of bleeding-heart , the pink-and-snow of little , thorny , single , sweetbreathed early rose .
The Old Lady have no fear of discovery , for the flower that grow in her garden grow in every other Spencervale garden as well , include the Stewart garden .
Chris Stewart , when he be tease about the music teacher , merely smile and hold his peace .
Chris know perfectly well who be the real giver of those flower .
He have make it his business to find out when the Mayflower gossip start .
But since it be evident Old Lady Lloyd do not wish it to be know , Chris tell no one .
Chris have always like Old Lady Lloyd ever since the day , ten year before , when she have find him cry in the wood with a cut foot and have take him into her house , and bath and bind the wound , and give him ten cent to buy candy at the store .
The Old Lady go without supper that night because of it , but Chris never know that .
The Old Lady think it a most beautiful June .
She no longer hat the new day ; on the contrary , she welcome them .
`` Every day be an uncommon day now , '' she say jubilantly to herself -- for do not almost every day bring her a glimpse of Sylvia ?
Even on rainy day the Old Lady gallantly brave rheumatism to hide behind her clump of drip spruce and watch Sylvia pas .
The only day she could not see her be Sundays ; and no Sundays have ever seem so long to Old Lady Lloyd a those June Sundays do .
One day the egg pedlar have news for her .
`` The music teacher be go to sing a solo for a collection piece to-morrow , '' he tell her .
The Old Lady 's black eye flash with interest .
`` I do n't know Miss Gray be a member of the choir , '' she say .
`` Jined two Sundays ago .
I tell you , our music be something worth listen to now .
The church 'll be pack to-morrow , I reckon -- her name 's go all over the country for sing .
You ought to come and hear it , Miss Lloyd . ''
The pedlar say this out of bravado , merely to show he be n't scar of the Old Lady , for all her grand air .
The Old Lady make no answer , and he think he have offend her .
He go away , wish he have n't say it .
Had he but know it , the Old Lady have forget the existence of all and any egg pedlar .
He have blot himself and his insignificance out of her consciousness by his last sentence .
All her thought , feeling , and wish be submerge in a very whirlpool of desire to hear Sylvia sing that solo .
She go into the house in a tumult and try to conquer that desire .
She could not do it , even think she summon all her pride to her aid .
Pride say : `` You will have to go to church to hear her .
You have n't fit clothes to go to church in .
Think what a figure you will make before them all . ''
But , for the first time , a more insistent voice than pride speak to her soul -- and , for the first time , the Old Lady listen to it .
It be too true that she have never go to church since the day on which she have to begin wear her mother 's silk dress .
The Old Lady herself think that this be very wicked ; and she try to atone by keep Sunday very strictly , and always have a little service of her own , morning and evening .
She sing three hymn in her cracked voice , pray aloud , and read a sermon .
But she could not bring herself to go to church in her out-of-date clothes -- she , who have once set the fashion in Spencervale , and the long she stay away , the more impossible it seem that she should ever again go .
Now the impossible have become , not only possible , but insistent .
She must go to church and hear Sylvia sing , no matter how ridiculous she appear , no matter how people talk and laugh at her .
Spencervale congregation have a mild sensation the next afternoon .
Just before the opening of service Old Lady Lloyd walk up the aisle and sit down in the long-unoccupied Lloyd pew , in front of the pulpit .
The Old Lady 's very soul be writhe within her .
She recall the reflection she have see in her mirror before she leave -- the old black silk in the mode of thirty year agone and the queer little bonnet of shirred black satin .
She think how absurd she must look in the eye of her world .
As a matter of fact , she do not look in the least absurd .
Some woman might have ; but the Old Lady 's stately distinction of carriage and figure be so subtly command that it do away with the consideration of garment altogether .
The Old Lady do not know this .
But she do know that Mrs. Kimball , the storekeeper 's wife , presently rustle into the next pew in the very late fashion of fabric and mode ; she and Mrs. Kimball be the same age , and there have be a time when the latter have be content to imitate Margaret Lloyd 's costume at a humble distance .
But the storekeeper have propose , and thing be change now ; and there sat poor Old Lady Lloyd , feel the change bitterly , and half wish she have not come to church at all .
Then all at once the Angel of Love touch there foolish thought , born of vanity and morbid pride , and they melt away a if they have never be .
Sylvia Gray have come into the choir , and be sit just where the afternoon sunshine fell over her beautiful hair like a halo .
The Old Lady look at her in a rapture of satisfied longing and thenceforth the service be bless to her , a anything be bless which come through the medium of unselfish love , whether human or divine .
Nay , be they not one and the same , differ in degree only , not in kind ?
The Old Lady have never have such a good , satisfying look at Sylvia before .
All her former glimpse have be steal and fleet .
Now she sit and gazed upon her to her hungry heart 's content , linger delightedly over every little charm and loveliness -- the way Sylvia 's shin hair ripple back from her forehead , the sweet little trick she have of drop quickly her long-lashed eyelid when she encounter too bold or curious a glance , and the slender , beautifully model hand -- so like Leslie Gray 's hand -- that hold her hymn book .
She be dress very plainly in a black skirt and a white shirtwaist ; but none of the other girl in the choir , with all their fine feather , could hold a candle to her -- a the egg pedlar say to his wife , go home from church .
The Old Lady listen to the opening hymn with keen pleasure .
Sylvia 's voice thrill through and dominate them all .
But when the usher get up to take the collection , an undercurrent of subdued excitement flow over the congregation .
Sylvia rise and come forward to Janet Moore 's side at the organ .
The next moment her beautiful voice soar through the building like the very soul of melody -- true , clear , powerful , sweet .
Nobody in Spencervale have ever listen to such a voice , except Old Lady Lloyd herself , who , in her youth , have hear enough good singing to enable her to be a tolerable judge of it .
She realize instantly that this girl of her heart have a great gift -- a gift that would some day bring her fame and fortune , if it could be duly trained and develop .
`` Oh , I 'm so glad I come to church , '' think Old Lady Lloyd .
When the solo be end , the Old Lady 's conscience compel her to drag her eye and thought from Sylvia , and fasten them on the minister , who have be flatter himself all through the opening portion of the service that Old Lady Lloyd have come to church on his account .
He be newly settle , have be in charge of the Spencervale congregation only a few month ; he be a clever little fellow and he honestly think it be the fame of his preaching that have bring Old Lady Lloyd out to church .
When the service be over all the Old Lady 's neighbour come to speak to her , with kindly smile and handshake .
They think they ought to encourage her , now that she have make a start in the right direction ; the Old Lady like their cordiality , and like it none the le because she detect in it the same unconscious respect and deference she have be wont to receive in the old day -- a respect and deference which her personality compel from all who approach her .
The Old Lady be surprise to find that she could command it still , in defiance of unfashionable bonnet and ancient attire .
Janet Moore and Sylvia Gray walk home from church together .
`` Did you see Old Lady Lloyd out to-day ? ''
ask Janet .
`` I be amaze when she walk in .
She have never be to church in my recollection .
What a quaint old figure she be !
She 's very rich , you know , but she wear her mother 's old clothes and never get a new thing .
Some people think she be mean ; but , '' conclude Janet charitably , `` I believe it be simply eccentricity . ''
`` I felt that be Miss Lloyd as soon a I saw her , although I have never see her before , '' say Sylvia dreamily .
`` I have be wish to see her -- for a certain reason .
She have a very striking face .
I should like to meet her -- to know her . ''
`` I do n't think it 's likely you ever will , '' say Janet carelessly .
`` She do n't like young people and she never go anywhere .
I do n't think I 'd like to know her .
I 'd be afraid of her -- she have such stately way and such strange , pierce eye . '' ''
I should n't be afraid of her , '' say Sylvia to herself , a she turn into the Spencer lane .
`` But I do n't expect I 'll ever become acquainted with her .
If she know who I be I suppose she would dislike me .
I suppose she never suspect that I be Leslie Gray 's daughter . ''
The minister , think it well to strike while the iron be hot , go up to call on Old Lady Lloyd the very next afternoon .
He go in fear and trembling , for he have hear thing about Old Lady Lloyd ; but she make herself so agreeable in her high-bred fashion that he be delight , and tell his wife when he go home that Spencervale people do n't understand Miss Lloyd .
This be perfectly true ; but it be by no mean certain that the minister understand her either .
He make only one mistake in tact , but , a the Old Lady do not snub him for it , he never know he make it .
When he be leave he say , `` I hope we shall see you at church next Sunday , Miss Lloyd . ''
`` Indeed , you will , '' say the Old Lady emphatically .
III .
The July Chapter The first day of July Sylvia find a little birch bark boat full of strawberry at the beech in the hollow .
They be the early of the season ; the Old Lady have find them in one of her secret haunt .
They would have be a toothsome addition to the Old Lady 's own slender bill of fare ; but she never think of eat them .
She get far more pleasure out of the thought of Sylvia 's enjoy them for her tea .
Thereafter the strawberry alternate with the flower as long a they last , and then come blueberry and raspberry .
The blueberry grow far away and the Old Lady have many a tramp after them .
Sometimes her bone ache at night because of it ; but what care the Old Lady for that ?
Bone ache be easy to endure than soul ache ; and the Old Lady 's soul have stop ache for the first time in many year .
It be be nourish with heavenly manna .
One evening Crooked Jack come up to fix something that have go wrong with the Old Lady 's well .
The Old Lady wander affably out to him ; for she know he have be work at the Spencers ' all day , and there might be crumb of information about Sylvia to be pick up .
`` I reckon the music teacher 's feeling pretty blue this evening , '' Crooked Jack remark , after strain the Old Lady 's patience to the last verge of human endurance by expatiate on William Spencer 's new pump , and Mrs. Spencer 's new washing-machine , and Amelia Spencer 's new young man .
`` Why ? ''
ask the Old Lady , turn very pale .
Had anything happen to Sylvia ?
`` Well , she 's be invite to a big party at Mrs. Moore 's brother 's in town , and she have n't get a dress to go in , '' say Crooked Jack .
`` They 're great swell and everybody will be get up regardless .
Mrs. Spencer be tell me about it .
She say Miss Gray ca n't afford a new dress because she 's help to pay her aunt 's doctor 's bill .
She say she 's sure Miss Gray feel awful disappoint over it , though she do n't let on .
But Mrs. Spencer say she know she be cry after she go to bed last night . ''
The Old Lady turn and go into the house abruptly .
This be dreadful .
Sylvia must go to that party -- she MUST .
But how be it to be manage ?
Through the Old Lady 's brain pass wild thought of her mother 's silk dress .
But none of them would be suitable , even if there be time to make one over .
Never have the Old Lady so bitterly regret her vanish wealth .
`` I 've only two dollar in the house , '' she say , `` and I 've get to live on that till the next day the egg pedlar come round .
Is there anything I can sell -- ANYTHING ?
Yes , yes , the grape jug ! ''
Up to this time , the Old Lady would as soon have think of try to sell her head a the grape jug .
The grape jug be two hundred year old and have be in the Lloyd family ever since it be a jug at all .
It be a big , pot-bellied affair , festoon with pink-gilt grape , and with a verse of poetry print on one side , and it have be give a a wedding present to the Old Lady 's great-grandmother .
As long a the Old Lady could remember it have sit on the top shelf in the cupboard in the sitting-room wall , far too precious ever to be use .
Two year before , a woman who collect old china have explore Spencervale , and , get word of the grape jug , have boldly invade the old Lloyd place and offer to buy it .
She never , to her die day , forget the reception the Old Lady give her ; but , be wise in her day and generation , she leave her card , say that if Miss Lloyd ever change her mind about sell the jug , she would find that she , the aforesaid collector , have not change hers about buy it .
People who make a hobby of heirloom china must meekly overlook snub , and this particular person have never see anything she covet so much a that grape jug .
The Old Lady have tear the card to piece ; but she remember the name and address .
She go to the cupboard and take down the beloved jug .
`` I never think to part with it , '' she say wistfully , `` but Sylvia must have a dress , and there be no other way .
And , after all , when I 'm go , who would there be to have it ?
Strangers would get it then -- it might as well go to them now .
I 'll have to go to town to-morrow morning , for there 's no time to lose if the party be Friday night .
I have n't be to town for ten year .
I dread the thought of go , more than part with the jug .
But for Sylvia 's sake ! ''
It be all over Spencervale by the next morning that Old Lady Lloyd have go to town , carry a carefully guard box .
Everybody wonder why she go ; most people suppose she have become too frightened to keep her money in a black box below her bed , when there have be two burglary over at Carmody , and have take it to the bank .
The Old Lady seek out the address of the china collector , tremble with fear that she might be dead or go .
But the collector be there , very much alive , and a keenly anxious to possess the grape jug a ever .
The Old Lady , pallid with the pain of her trampled pride , sell the grape jug and go away , believe that her great-grandmother must have turn over in her grave at the moment of the transaction .
Old Lady Lloyd felt like a traitor to her tradition .
But she go unflinchingly to a big store and , guide by that special Providence which look after simple-minded old soul in their dangerous excursion into the world , find a sympathetic clerk who know just what she want and get it for her .
The Old Lady select a very dainty muslin gown , with glove and slipper in keep ; and she order it send at once , expressage prepaid , to Miss Sylvia Gray , in care of William Spencer , Spencervale .
Then she pay down the money -- the whole price of the jug , minus a dollar and a half for railroad fare -- with a grand , careless air and depart .
As she march erectly down the aisle of the store , she encounter a sleek , portly , prosperous man come in .
As their eye meet , the man start and his bland face flush crimson ; he lift his hat and bow confusedly .
But the Old Lady look through him a if he be n't there , and pass on with not a sign of recognition about her .
He take one step after her , then stop and turn away , with a rather disagreeable smile and a shrug of his shoulder .
Nobody would have guess , a the Old Lady sweep out , how her heart be seethe with abhorrence and scorn .
She would not have have the courage to come to town , even for Sylvia 's sake , if she have think she would meet Andrew Cameron .
The mere sight of him open up anew a sealed fountain of bitterness in her soul ; but the thought of Sylvia somehow stem the torrent , and presently the Old Lady be smile rather triumphantly , think rightly that she have come off best in that unwelcome encounter .
SHE , at any rate , have not falter and colour , and lose her presence of mind .
`` It be little wonder HE do , '' think the Old Lady vindictively .
It please her that Andrew Cameron should lose , before her , the front of adamant he present to the world .
He be her cousin and the only living creature Old Lady Lloyd hat , and she hat and despise him with all the intensity of her intense nature .
She and hers have sustain grievous wrong at his hand , and the Old Lady be convince that she would rather die than take any notice of his existence .
Presently , she resolutely put Andrew Cameron out of her mind .
It be desecration to think of him and Sylvia together .
When she lay her weary head on her pillow that night she be so happy that even the thought of the vacant shelf in the room below , where the grape jug have always be , give her only a momentary pang .
`` It 's sweet to sacrifice for one we love -- it 's sweet to have someone to sacrifice for , '' think the Old Lady .
Desire grows by what it feed on .
The Old Lady think she be content ; but Friday evening come and find her in a perfect fever to see Sylvia in her party dress .
It be not enough to fancy her in it ; nothing would do the Old Lady but see her .
`` And I SHALL see her , '' say the Old Lady resolutely , look out from her window at Sylvia 's light gleam through the fir .
She wrap herself in a dark shawl and creep out , slip down to the hollow and up the wood lane .
It be a misty , moonlight night , and a wind , fragrant with the aroma of clover field , blow down the lane to meet her .
`` I wish I could take your perfume -- the soul of you -- and pour it into her life , '' say the Old Lady aloud to that wind .
Sylvia Gray be stand in her room , ready for the party .
Before her stood Mrs. Spencer and Amelia Spencer and all the little Spencer girl , in an admiring semi-circle .
There be another spectator .
Outside , under the lilac bush , Old Lady Lloyd be stand .
She could see Sylvia plainly , in her dainty dress , with the pale pink roses Old Lady Lloyd have leave at the beech that day for her in her hair .
Pink a they be , they be not so pink a her cheek , and her eye shine like star .
Amelia Spencer put up her hand to push back a rise that have fall a little out of place , and the Old Lady envy her fiercely .
`` That dress could n't have fit good if it have be make for you , '' say Mrs. Spencer admiringly .
`` Ai n't she lovely , Amelia ?
Who COULD have send it ? ''
`` Oh , I feel sure that Mrs. Moore be the fairy godmother , '' say Sylvia .
`` There be nobody else who would .
It be dear of her -- she know I wish so much to go to the party with Janet .
I wish Aunty could see me now . ''
Sylvia give a little sigh in spite of her joy .
`` There 's nobody else to care very much . ''
Ah , Sylvia , you be wrong !
There be somebody else -- somebody who care very much -- an Old Lady , with eager , devour eye , who be stand under the lilac bush and who presently steal away through the moonlit orchard to the wood like a shadow , go home with a vision of you in your girlish beauty to companion her through the watch of that summer night .
IV .
The August Chapter One day the minister 's wife rush in where Spencervale people have fear to tread , go boldly to Old Lady Lloyd , and ask her if she would n't come to their Sewing Circle , which meet fortnightly on Saturday afternoon .
`` We be fill a box to send to our Trinidad missionary , '' say the minister 's wife , `` and we should be so pleased to have you come , Miss Lloyd . ''
The Old Lady be on the point of refuse rather haughtily .
Not that she be oppose to mission -- or sew circle either -- quite the contrary , but she know that each member of the Circle be expect to pay ten cent a week for the purpose of procure sew material ; and the poor Old Lady really do not see how she could afford it .
But a sudden thought check her refusal before it reach her lip .
`` I suppose some of the young girl go to the Circle ? ''
she say craftily .
`` Oh , they all go , '' say the minister 's wife .
`` Janet Moore and Miss Gray be our most enthusiastic member .
It be very lovely of Miss Gray to give her Saturday afternoon -- the only one she have free from pupil -- to our work .
But she really have the sweet disposition . ''
`` I 'll join your Circle , '' say the Old Lady promptly .
She be determine she would do it , if she have to live on two meal a day to save the necessary fee .
She go to the Sewing Circle at James Martin 's the next Saturday , and do the most beautiful hand sew for them .
She be so expert at it that she do n't need to think about it at all , which be rather fortunate , for all her thought be take up with Sylvia , who sit in the opposite corner with Janet Moore , her graceful hand busy with a little boy 's coarse gingham shirt .
Nobody thought of introduce Sylvia to Old Lady Lloyd , and the Old Lady be glad of it .
She sew finely away , and listen with all her ear to the girlish chatter which go on in the opposite corner .
One thing she find out -- Sylvia 's birthday be the twentieth of August .
And the Old Lady be straightway fire with a consume wish to give Sylvia a birthday present .
She lay awake most of the night wonder if she could do it , and most sorrowfully conclude that it be utterly out of the question , no matter how she might pinch and contrive .
Old Lady Lloyd worry quite absurdly over this , and it haunt her like a spectre until the next Sewing Circle day .
It meet at Mrs. Moore 's and Mrs. Moore be especially gracious to Old Lady Lloyd , and insist on her take the wicker rocker in the parlour .
The Old Lady would rather have be in the sitting-room with the young girl , but she submit for courtesy 's sake -- and she have her reward .
Her chair be just behind the parlour door , and presently Janet Moore and Sylvia Gray come and sit on the stair in the hall outside , where a cool breeze blew in through the maple before the front door .
They be talk of their favourite poet .
Janet , it appear , adore Byron and Scott .
Sylvia lean to Tennyson and Browning .
`` Do you know , '' say Sylvia softly , `` my father be a poet ?
He publish a little volume of verse once ; and , Janet , I 've never see a copy of it , and oh , how I would love to !
It be publish when he be at college -- just a small , private edition to give his friend .
He never publish any more -- poor father !
I think life disappoint him .
But I have such a longing to see that little book of his verse .
I have n't a scrap of his writing .
If I have it would seem a if I possess something of him -- of his heart , his soul , his inner life .
He would be something more than a mere name to me . ''
`` Did n't he have a copy of his own -- do n't your mother have one ? ''
ask Janet .
`` Mother have n't .
She die when I be bear , you know , but Aunty say there be no copy of father 's poem among mother 's book .
Mother do n't care for poetry , Aunty say -- Aunty do n't either .
Father go to Europe after mother die , and he die there the next year .
Nothing that he have with him be ever send home to u .
He have sell most of his book before he go , but he give a few of his favourite one to Aunty to keep for me .
HIS book be n't among them .
I do n't suppose I shall ever find a copy , but I should be so delighted if I only could . ''
When the Old Lady get home she take from her top bureau drawer an inlaid box of sandalwood .
It hold a little , slim , limp volume , wrap in tissue paper -- the Old Lady 's most treasured possession .
On the fly-leaf be write , `` To Margaret , with the author 's love . ''
The Old Lady turn the yellow leaf with tremble finger and , through eye brim with tear , read the verse , although she have know them all by heart for year .
She mean to give the book to Sylvia for a birthday present -- one of the most precious gift ever give , if the value of gift be gauge by the measure of self-sacrifice involve .
In that little book be immortal love -- old laughter -- old tear -- old beauty which have bloom like a rise year ago , hold still it sweetness like old rise leaf .
She remove the telltale fly-leaf ; and late on the night before Sylvia 's birthday , the Old Lady crept , under cover of the darkness , through byway and across field , a if bent on some nefarious expedition , to the little Spencervale store where the post-office be keep .
She slip the thin parcel through the slit in the door , and then steal home again , feel a strange sense of loss and loneliness .
It be a if she have give away the last link between herself and her youth .
But she do not regret it .
It would give Sylvia pleasure , and that have come to be the overmastering passion of the Old Lady 's heart .
The next night the light in Sylvia 's room burn very late , and the Old Lady watch it triumphantly , know the meaning of it .
Sylvia be read her father 's poem , and the Old Lady in her darkness read them too , murmur the line over and over to herself .
After all , give away the book have not matter so very much .
She have the soul of it still -- and the fly-leaf with the name , in Leslie 's writing , by which nobody ever call her now .
The Old Lady be sit on the Marshall sofa the next Sewing Circle afternoon when Sylvia Gray come and sit down beside her .
The Old Lady 's hand tremble a little , and one side of a handkerchief , which be afterwards give a a Christmas present to a little olive-skinned coolie in Trinidad , be not quite so exquisitely do a the other three side .
Sylvia at first talked of the Circle , and Mrs. Marshall 's dahlia , and the Old Lady be in the seventh heaven of delight , though she take care not to show it , and be even a little more stately and finely mannered than usual .
When she ask Sylvia how she like live in Spencervale , Sylvia say , `` Very much .
Everybody be so kind to me .
Besides '' -- Sylvia lower her voice so that nobody but the Old Lady could hear it -- `` I have a fairy godmother here who do the most beautiful and wonderful thing for me . ''
Sylvia , be a girl of fine instinct , do not look at Old Lady Lloyd a she say this .
But she would not have see anything if she have look .
The Old Lady be not a Lloyd for nothing .
`` How very interesting , '' she say , indifferently .
`` Is n't it ?
I be so grateful to her and I have wish so much she might know how much pleasure she have give me .
I have find lovely flower and delicious berry on my path all summer ; I feel sure she send me my party dress .
But the dear gift come last week on my birthday -- a little volume of my father 's poem .
I ca n't express what I felt on receive them .
But I long to meet my fairy godmother and thank her . ''
`` Quite a fascinating mystery , be n't it ?
Have you really no idea who she be ? ''
The Old Lady ask this dangerous question with marked success .
She would not have be so successful if she have not be so sure that Sylvia have no idea of the old romance between her and Leslie Gray .
As it be , she have a comfortable conviction that she herself be the very last person Sylvia would be likely to suspect .
Sylvia hesitate for an almost unnoticeable moment .
Then she say , `` I have n't try to find out , because I do n't think she want me to know .
At first , of course , in the matter of the flower and dress , I do try to solve the mystery ; but , since I receive the book , I become convinced that it be my fairy godmother who be do it all , and I have respect her wish for concealment and always shall .
Perhaps some day she will reveal herself to me .
I hope so , at least . ''
`` I would n't hope it , '' say the Old Lady discouragingly .
`` Fairy godmother -- at least , in all the fairy tale I ever read -- be somewhat apt to be queer , crochety people , much more agreeable when wrap up in mystery than when met face to face . ''
`` I 'm convinced that mine be the very opposite , and that the good I become acquainted with her , the more charm a personage I should find her , '' say Sylvia gaily .
Mrs. Marshall come up at this juncture and entreat Miss Gray to sing for them .
Miss Gray consent sweetly , the Old Lady be leave alone and be rather glad of it .
She enjoy her conversation with Sylvia much more in think it over after she get home than while it be take place .
When an Old Lady have a guilty conscience , it be apt to make her nervous and distract her thought from immediate pleasure .
She wonder a little uneasily if Sylvia really do suspect her .
Then she conclude that it be out of the question .
Who would suspect a mean , unsociable Old Lady , who have no friend , and who give only five cent to the Sewing Circle when everyone else give ten or fifteen , to be a fairy godmother , the donor of beautiful party dress , and the recipient of gift from romantic , aspire young poet ?
V .
The September Chapter In September the Old Lady look back on the summer and own to herself that it have be a strangely happy one , with Sundays and Sewing Circle day stand out like golden punctuation mark in a poem of life .
She felt like an utterly different woman ; and other people think her different also .
The Sewing Circle woman find her so pleasant , and even friendly , that they begin to think they have misjudge her , and that perhaps it be eccentricity after all , and not meanness , which account for her peculiar mode of living .
Sylvia Gray always come and talk to her on Circle afternoon now , and the Old Lady treasure every word she say in her heart and repeat them over and over to her lonely self in the watch of the night .
Sylvia never talk of herself or her plan , unless ask about them ; and the Old Lady 's self-consciousness prevent her from ask any personal question : so their conversation keep to the surface of thing , and it be not from Sylvia , but from the minister 's wife that the Old Lady finally discover what her darling 's dear ambition be .
The minister 's wife have drop in at the old Lloyd place one evening late in September , when a chilly wind be blow up from the northeast and moan about the eaves of the house , a if the burden of it lay be `` harvest be end and summer be go . ''
The Old Lady have be listen to it , a she plait a little basket of sweet grass for Sylvia .
She have walk all the way to Avonlea sand-hills for it the day before , and she be very tired .
And her heart be sad .
This summer , which have so enrich her life , be almost over ; and she know that Sylvia Gray talk of leave Spencervale at the end of October .
The Old Lady 's heart felt like very lead within her at the thought , and she almost welcome the advent of the minister 's wife a a distraction , although she be desperately afraid that the minister 's wife have call to ask for a subscription for the new vestry carpet , and the Old Lady simply could not afford to give one cent .
But the minister 's wife have merely drop in on her way home from the Spencers ' and she do not make any embarrassing request .
Instead , she talk about Sylvia Gray , and her word fell on the Old Lady 's ear like separate pearl note of unutterably sweet music .
The minister 's wife have nothing but praise for Sylvia -- she be so sweet and beautiful and win .
`` And with SUCH a voice , '' say the minister 's wife enthusiastically , add with a sigh , `` It 's such a shame she ca n't have it properly train .
She would certainly become a great singer -- competent critic have tell her so .
But she be so poor she do n't think she can ever possibly manage it -- unless she can get one of the Cameron scholarship , a they be call ; and she have very little hope of that , although the professor of music who teach her have send her name in . ''
`` What be the Cameron scholarship ? ''
ask the Old Lady .
`` Well , I suppose you have hear of Andrew Cameron , the millionaire ? ''
say the minister 's wife , serenely unconscious that she be cause the very bone of the Old Lady 's family skeleton to jangle in their closet .
Into the Old Lady 's white face come a sudden faint stain of colour , a if a rough hand have strike her cheek .
`` Yes , I 've hear of him , '' she say .
`` Well , it seem that he have a daughter , who be a very beautiful girl , and whom he idolize .
She have a fine voice , and he be go to send her abroad to have it train .
And she die .
It nearly break his heart , I understand .
But ever since , he send one young girl away to Europe every year for a thorough musical education under the best teacher -- in memory of his daughter .
He have send nine or ten already ; but I fear there be n't much chance for Sylvia Gray , and she do n't think there be herself . ''
`` Why not ? ''
ask the Old Lady spiritedly .
`` I be sure that there can be few voice equal to Miss Gray 's . ''
`` Very true .
But you see , these so-called scholarship be private affair , dependent solely on the whim and choice of Andrew Cameron himself .
Of course , when a girl have friend who use their influence with him , he will often send her on their recommendation .
They say he send a girl last year who have n't much of a voice at all just because her father have be an old business crony of his .
But Sylvia do n't know anyone at all who would , to use a slang term , have any ` pull ' with Andrew Cameron , and she be not acquaint with him herself .
Well , I must be go ; we 'll see you at the Manse on Saturday , I hope , Miss Lloyd .
The Circle meet there , you know . ''
`` Yes , I know , '' say the Old Lady absently .
When the minister 's wife have go , she drop her sweetgrass basket and sat for a long , long time with her hand lie idly in her lap , and her big black eye star unseeingly at the wall before her .
Old Lady Lloyd , so pitifully poor that she have to eat six cracker the less a week to pay her fee to the Sewing Circle , know that it be in her power -- HERS -- to send Leslie Gray 's daughter to Europe for her musical education !
If she choose to use her `` pull '' with Andrew Cameron -- if she go to him and ask him to send Sylvia Gray abroad the next year -- she have no doubt whatever that it would be do .
It all lay with her -- if -- if -- IF she could so far crush and conquer her pride a to stoop to ask a favour of the man who have wrong her and hers so bitterly .
Years ago , her father , act under the advice and urgency of Andrew Cameron , have invest all his little fortune in an enterprise that have turn out a failure .
Abraham Lloyd lose every dollar he possess , and his family be reduce to utter poverty .
Andrew Cameron might have be forgive for a mistake ; but there be a strong suspicion , amount to almost certainty , that he have be guilty of something far bad than a mistake in regard to his uncle 's investment .
Nothing could be legally prove ; but it be certain that Andrew Cameron , already note for his `` sharp practice , '' emerge with improved finance from an entanglement that have ruin many good men ; and old Doctor Lloyd have die brokenhearted , believe that his nephew have deliberately victimize him .
Andrew Cameron have not quite do this ; he have mean well enough by his uncle at first , and what he have finally do he try to justify to himself by the doctrine that a man must look out for Number One .
Margaret Lloyd make no such excuse for him ; she hold him responsible , not only for her lose fortune , but for her father 's death , and never forgive him for it .
When Abraham Lloyd have die , Andrew Cameron , perhaps prick by his conscience , have come to her , sleekly and smoothly , to offer her financial aid .
He would see , he tell her , that she never suffer want .
Margaret Lloyd fling his offer back in his face after a fashion that leave nothing to be desire in the way of plain speaking .
She would die , she tell him passionately , before she would accept a penny or a favour from him .
He have preserve an unbroken show of good temper , express his heartfelt regret that she should cherish such an unjust opinion of him , and have leave her with an oily assurance that he would always be her friend , and would always be delight to render her any assistance in his power whenever she should choose to ask for it .
The Old Lady have live for twenty year in the firm conviction that she would die in the poorhouse -- a , indeed , seem not unlikely -- before she would ask a favour of Andrew Cameron .
And so , in truth , she would have , have it be for herself .
But for Sylvia !
Could she so far humble herself for Sylvia 's sake ?
The question be not easily or speedily settle , a have be the case in the matter of the grape jug and the book of poem .
For a whole week the Old Lady fight her pride and bitterness .
Sometimes , in the hour of sleepless night , when all human resentment and rancour seem petty and contemptible , she think she have conquer it .
But in the daytime , with the picture of her father look down at her from the wall , and the rustle of her unfashionable dress , worn because of Andrew Cameron 's double dealing , in her ear , it get the good of her again .
But the Old Lady 's love for Sylvia have grow so strong and deep and tender that no other feeling could endure finally against it .
Love be a great miracle worker ; and never have it power be more strongly make manifest than on the cold , dull autumn morning when the Old Lady walk to Bright River railway station and take the train to Charlottetown , bent on an errand the very thought of which turn her soul sick within her .
The station master who sell her her ticket think Old Lady Lloyd look uncommonly white and peak -- `` a if she have n't sleep a wink or eat a bite for a week , '' he tell his wife at dinner time .
`` Guess there 's something wrong in her business affair .
This be the second time she 's go to town this summer . ''
When the Old Lady reach the town , she eat her slender little lunch and then walk out to the suburb where the Cameron factory and warehouse be .
It be a long walk for her , but she could not afford to drive .
She felt very tired when she be show into the shining , luxurious office where Andrew Cameron sit at his desk .
After the first startle glance of surprise , he come forward beamingly , with outstretched hand .
`` Why , Cousin Margaret !
This be a pleasant surprise .
Sit down -- allow me , this be a much more comfortable chair .
Did you come in this morning ?
And how be everybody out in Spencervale ? ''
The Old Lady have flush at his first word .
To hear the name by which her father and mother and lover have call her on Andrew Cameron 's lip seem like profanation .
But , she tell herself , the time be past for squeamishness .
If she could ask a favour of Andrew Cameron , she could bear less pang .
For Sylvia 's sake she shake hand with him , for Sylvia 's sake she sit down in the chair he offer .
But for no living human be 's sake could this determine Old Lady infuse any cordiality into her manner or her word .
She go straight to the point with Lloyd simplicity .
`` I have come to ask a favour of you , '' she say , look him in the eye , not at all humbly or meekly , a become a suppliant , but challengingly and defiantly , a if she dare him to refuse .
`` DE-lighted to hear it , Cousin Margaret . ''
Never be anything so bland and gracious a his tone .
`` Anything I can do for you I shall be only too pleased to do .
I be afraid you have look upon me a an enemy , Margaret , and I assure you I have felt your injustice keenly .
I realize that some appearance be against me , but -- '' The Old Lady lift her hand and stem his eloquence by that one gesture .
`` I do not come here to discuss that matter , '' she say .
`` We will not refer to the past , if you please .
I come to ask a favour , not for myself , but for a very dear young friend of mine -- a Miss Gray , who have a remarkably fine voice which she wish to have train .
She be poor , so I come to ask you if you would give her one of your musical scholarship .
I understand her name have already be suggest to you , with a recommendation from her teacher .
I do not know what he have say of her voice , but I do know he could hardly overrate it .
If you send her abroad for training , you will not make any mistake . ''
The Old Lady stop talk .
She felt sure Andrew Cameron would grant her request , but she do hope he would grant it rather rudely or unwillingly .
She could accept the favour so much more easily if it be flung to her like a bone to a dog .
But not a bit of it .
Andrew Cameron be suaver than ever .
Nothing could give him great pleasure than to grant his dear Cousin Margaret 's request -- he only wish it involve more trouble on his part .
Her little protege should have her musical education assuredly -- she should go abroad next year -- and he be DE-lighted -- `` Thank you , '' say the Old Lady , cut him short again .
`` I be much oblige to you -- and I ask you not to let Miss Gray know anything of my interference .
And I shall not take up any more of your valuable time .
Good afternoon . ''
`` Oh , you must n't go so soon , '' he say , with some real kindness or clannishness permeate the hateful cordiality of his voice -- for Andrew Cameron be not entirely without the homely virtue of the average man .
He have be a good husband and father ; he have once be very fond of his Cousin Margaret ; and he be really very sorry that `` circumstance '' have `` compel '' him to act a he have do in that old affair of her father 's investment .
`` You must be my guest to-night . ''
`` Thank you .
I must return home to-night , '' say the Old Lady firmly , and there be that in her tone which tell Andrew Cameron that it would be useless to urge her .
But he insist on telephone for his carriage to drive her to the station .
The Old Lady submit to this , because she be secretly afraid her own leg would not suffice to carry her there ; she even shake hand with him at parting , and thank him a second time for grant her request .
`` Not at all , '' he say .
`` Please try to think a little more kindly of me , Cousin Margaret . ''
When the Old Lady reach the station she find , to her dismay , that her train have just go and that she would have to wait two hour for the even one .
She go into the waiting-room and sit down .
She be very tired .
All the excitement that have sustain her be go , and she felt weak and old .
She have nothing to eat , have expect to get home in time for tea ; the waiting-room be chilly , and she shiver in her thin , old , silk mantilla .
Her head ache and her heart likewise .
She have win Sylvia 's desire for her ; but Sylvia would go out of her life , and the Old Lady do not see how she be to go on living after that .
Yet she sit there unflinchingly for two hour , an upright , indomitable old figure , silently fight her losing battle with the force of physical and mental pain , while happy people come and go , and laugh and talk before her .
At eight o'clock the Old Lady get off the train at Bright River station , and slip off unnoticed into the darkness of the wet night .
She have two mile to walk , and a cold rain be fall .
Soon the Old Lady be wet to the skin and chill to the marrow .
She felt a if she be walk in a bad dream .
Blind instinct alone guide her over the last mile and up the lane to her own house .
As she fumble at her door , she realize that a burning heat have suddenly take the place of her chilliness .
She stumble in over her threshold and close the door .
VI .
The October Chapter On the second morning after Old Lady Lloyd 's journey to town , Sylvia Gray be walk blithely down the wood lane .
It be a beautiful autumn morning , clear and crisp and sunny ; the frosted fern , drench and batter with the rain of yesterday , give out a delicious fragrance ; here and there in the wood a maple wave a gay crimson banner , or a branch of birch show pale golden against the dark , unchanging spruce .
The air be very pure and exhilarating .
Sylvia walk with a joyous lightness of step and uplift of brow .
At the beech in the hollow she pause for an expectant moment , but there be nothing among the gray old root for her .
She be just turn away when little Teddy Kimball , who live next door to the manse , come run down the slope from the direction of the old Lloyd place .
Teddy 's freckled face be very pale .
`` Oh , Miss Gray ! ''
he gasp .
`` I guess Old Lady Lloyd have go clean crazy at last .
The minister 's wife ask me to run up to the Old Lady , with a message about the Sewing Circle -- and I knock -- and knock -- and nobody come -- so I think I 'd just step in and leave the letter on the table .
But when I open the door , I hear an awful queer laugh in the sitting-room , and next minute , the Old Lady come to the sitting-room door .
Oh , Miss Gray , she look awful .
Her face be red and her eye awful wild -- and she be mutter and talk to herself and laugh like mad .
I be so scared I just turn and run . ''
Sylvia , without stop for reflection , catch Teddy 's hand and run up the slope .
It do not occur to her to be frighten , although she think with Teddy that the poor , lonely , eccentric Old Lady have really go out of her mind at last .

Teddy , too frighten to go in , lurk on the step outside .
The Old Lady still wear the damp black silk dress in which she have walk from the station .
Her face be flush , her eye wild , her voice hoarse .
But she know Sylvia and cower down .

You 're to go to Europe -- Andrew Cameron be go to send you -- I ask him -- he could n't refuse ME .

Sylvia do not go away .
At a glance she have see that this be sickness and delirium , not insanity .

Several other woman offer assistance .
Everybody be kind and thoughtful .
But the Old Lady do not know it .
She do not even know Sylvia Gray , who come and sat by her every minute she could spare .

Everybody know now how poor the Old Lady really be .
She let slip all the jealously guarded secret of her existence , except her old love for Leslie Gray .
Even in delirium something seal her lip a to that .
But all else come out -- her anguish over her unfashionable attire , her pitiful makeshift and contrivance , her humiliation over wear unfashionable dress and pay only five cent where every other Sewing Circle member pay ten .
The kindly woman who wait on her listened to her with tear-filled eye , and repent of their harsh judgment in the past .
`` But who would have think it ? ''
say Mrs. Spencer to the minister 's wife .
`` Nobody ever dream that her father have lose ALL his money , though folk suppose he have lose some in that old affair of the silver mine out west .
It 's shock to think of the way she have live all these year , often with not enough to eat -- and go to bed in winter day to save fuel .
Though I suppose if we have know we could n't have do much for her , she 's so desperate proud .
But if she live , and will let u help her , thing will be different after this .
Crooked Jack say he 'll never forgive himself for take pay for the few little job he do for her .
He say , if she 'll only let him , he 'll do everything she want do for her after this for nothing .
Ai n't it strange what a fancy she 's take to Miss Gray ?
Think of her do all those thing for her all summer , and sell the grape jug and all .
Well , the Old Lady certainly be n't mean , but nobody make a mistake in call her queer .
It all do seem desperate pitiful .
Miss Gray 's take it awful hard .
She seem to think about as much of the Old Lady a the Old Lady think of her .
She 's so work up she do n't even seem to care about go to Europe next year .
She 's really go -- she 's have word from Andrew Cameron .
I 'm awful glad , for there never be a sweeter girl in the world ; but she say it will cost too much if the Old Lady 's life be to pay for it . ''
Andrew Cameron heard of the Old Lady 's illness and come out to Spencervale himself .
He be not allow to see the Old Lady , of course ; but he tell all concern that no expense or trouble be to be spar , and the Spencervale doctor be instruct to send his bill to Andrew Cameron and hold his peace about it .

III .
Each In His Own Tongue The honey-tinted autumn sunshine be fall thickly over the crimson and amber maple around old Abel Blair 's door .
There be only one outer door in old Abel 's house , and it almost always stand wide open .
A little black dog , with one ear miss and a lame forepaw , almost always sleep on the worn red sandstone slab which serve old Abel for a doorstep ; and on the still more worn sill above it a large gray cat almost always sleep .
Just inside the door , on a bandy-legged chair of elder day , old Abel almost always sit .
He be sit there this afternoon -- a little old man , sadly twist with rheumatism ; his head be abnormally large , thatch with long , wiry black hair ; his face be heavily line and swarthily sunburn ; his eye be deep-set and black , with occasional peculiar golden flash in them .
A strange look man be old Abel Blair ; and a strange be he a he look .
Lower Carmody people would have tell you .
Old Abel be almost always sober in these , his late year .
He be sober to-day .
He like to bask in that ripe sunlight as well a his dog and cat do ; and in such baskings he almost always look out of his doorway at the far , fine blue sky over the top of the crowding maple .
But to-day he be not look at the sky , instead , he be star at the black , dusty rafter of his kitchen , where hung dry meat and string of onion and bunch of herb and fishing tackle and gun and skin .
But old Abel saw not these thing ; his face be the face of a man who behold vision , compact of heavenly pleasure and hellish pain , for old Abel be see what he might have be -- and what he be ; a he always saw when Felix Moore play to him on the violin .
And the awful joy of dream that he be young again , with unspoiled life before him , be so great and compel that it counterbalance the agony in the realization of a dishonoured old age , follow year in which he have squander the wealth of his soul in way where Wisdom lift not her voice .

Under his chin he hold old Abel 's brown , batter fiddle ; his eye , too , be fix on the ceiling ; and he , too , saw thing not lawful to be utter in any language save that of music ; and of all music , only that give forth by the anguished , enraptured spirit of the violin .
And yet this Felix be little more than twelve year old , and his face be still the face of a child who know nothing of either sorrow or sin or failure or remorse .
Only in his large , gray-black eye be there something not of the child -- something that spoke of an inheritance from many heart , now ash , which have aforetime grieve and joy , and struggle and fail , and succeed and grovel .
The inarticulate cry of their longing have pass into this child 's soul , and transmute themselves into the expression of his music .
Felix be a beautiful child .
Carmody people , who stay at home , think so ; and old Abel Blair , who have roam afar in many land , think so ; and even the Rev . Stephen Leonard , who teach , and try to believe , that favour be deceitful and beauty be vain , think so .

He 's get a work to do -- if the minister 'll let him do it .
And if the minister do n't let him do it , then I would n't be in that minister 's shoe when he come to the judgment -- no , I 'd rather be in my own .
It 's an awful thing to cross the purpose of the Almighty , either in your own life or anybody else 's .
Sometimes I think it 's what 's mean by the unpardonable sin -- ay , that I do ! ''
Carmody people never ask what old Abel meant .
They have long ago give up such vain questioning .
When a man have live a old Abel have live for the great part of his life , be it any wonder he say crazy thing ?
And a for hint that Mr. Leonard , a man who be really almost too good to live , be guilty of any sin , much less an unpardonable one -- well , there now !
what use be it to be take any account of old Abel 's queer speech ?
Though , to be sure , there be no great harm in a fiddle , and maybe Mr. Leonard be a mite too strict that way with the child .
But then , could you wonder at it ?
There be his father , you see .

And to think you make it up yourself a you go along !

Felix shake his head .
`` I know he would n't , Abel .
He want me to be a minister .
Ministers be good thing to be , but I 'm afraid I ca n't be a minister . ''
`` Not a pulpit minister .
There 's different kind of minister , and each must talk to men in his own tongue if he 's go to do 'em any real good , '' say old Abel meditatively .
`` YOUR tongue be music .
Strange that your grandfather ca n't see that for himself , and him such a broad-minded man !
He 's the only minister I ever have much use for .
He 's God 's own if ever a man be .
And he love you -- yes , sir , he love you like the apple of his eye . ''

`` I love him so much that I 'll even try to be a minister for his sake , though I do n't want to be . ''

He teach me to play on it as soon a I be big enough to hold it . ''

I think he burn it .
And I long for it so often . ''

I do n't know how that be .

what do this mean ? ''

To Martin Moore 's outward mastery of the violin be add the mystery and intensity of his mother 's nature , with some more subtle quality still , which have perhaps come to him from the grandmother he so strongly resemble .
Moore have understand what a career be naturally before the child , and he have train him in the technique of his art from the time the slight finger could first grasp the bow .
When nine-year-old Felix come to the Carmody manse , he have master a much of the science of the violin a nine out of ten musician acquire in a lifetime ; and he bring with him his father 's violin ; it be all Martin Moore have to leave his son -- but it be an Amati , the commercial value of which nobody in Carmody suspect .
Mr. Leonard have take possession of it and Felix have never see it since .
He cry himself to sleep many a night for the loss of it .
Mr. Leonard do not know this , and if Janet Andrews suspect it she hold her tongue -- an art in which she excel .
She `` saw no harm in a fiddle , '' herself , and think Mr. Leonard absurdly strict in the matter , though it would not have be well for the luckless outsider who might have venture to say as much to her .

When Janet heard of the promise which Mr. Leonard have exact from Felix she seethe with indignation ; and , though she `` know her place '' good than to say anything to Mr. Leonard about it , she make her disapproval so plainly manifest in her bearing that the stern , gentle old man find the atmosphere of his hitherto peaceful manse unpleasantly chill and hostile for a time .
It be the wish of his heart that Felix should be a minister , a he would have wish his own son to be , have one be bear to him .
Mr. Leonard think rightly that the high work to which any man could be call be a life of service to his fellow ; but he make the mistake of suppose the field of service much narrow than it be -- of fail to see that a man may minister to the need of humanity in many different but equally effective way .
Janet hop that Mr. Leonard might not exact the fulfilment of Felix 's promise ; but Felix himself , with the instinctive understanding of perfect love , know that it be vain to hope for any change of viewpoint in his grandfather .
He address himself to the keep of his promise in letter and in spirit .
He never go again to old Abel 's ; he do not even play on the organ , though this be not forbidden , because any music waken in him a passion of longing and ecstasy which demand expression with an intensity not to be borne .
He fling himself grimly into his study and con Latin and Greek verb with a persistency which soon place him at the head of all competitor .
Only once in the long winter do he come near to break his promise .
One evening , when March be melt into April , and the pulse of spring be stir under the lingering snow , he be walk home from school alone .
As he descend into the little hollow below the manse a lively lilt of music drift up to meet him .
It be only the product of a mouth-organ , manipulate by a little black-eyed , French-Canadian hire boy , sit on the fence by the brook ; but there be music in the ragged urchin and it come out through his simple toy .
It tingle over Felix from head to foot ; and , when Leon hold out the mouth-organ with a fraternal grin of invitation , he snatch at it a a famished creature might snatch at food .
Then , with it half-way to his lip , he pause .
True , it be only the violin he have promise never to touch ; but he felt that if he give way ever so little to the desire that be in him , it would sweep everything before it .
If he play on Leon Buote 's mouth-organ , there in that misty spring dale , he would go to old Abel 's that evening ; he KNEW he would go .

`` Child , what 's the matter with you ? ''
she cry .
`` Are you sick ?
Have you be scar ? ''
`` No , no .
Leave me alone , Janet , '' say Felix chokingly , dash up the stair to his own room .

Well , he have study hard all winter , and he be certainly grow very fast .

`` She have be ail all winter , and now she 's fast to her bed .
Mrs. Murphy say she believe the woman be die , but nobody dare tell her so .
She wo n't give in she 's sick , nor take medicine .
And there 's nobody to wait on her except that simple creature , Maggie Peterson . ''

`` Oh , yes , '' Felix reply , address himself to his cherry preserve with considerable gusto .
`` I be down at Spruce Cove one night last summer when a big thunderstorm come up .
I go to Naomi 's house for shelter .
The door be open , so I walk right in , because nobody answer my knock .
Naomi Clark be at the window , watch the cloud come up over the sea .

It be dreadful to see , like the storm , and yet I like to see it .

But I could n't .

`` I do n't know . ''

He be a man who never shirk what he believe to be his duty ; but duty have sometimes present itself to him in pleasanter guise than this summons to Naomi Clark 's death-bed .
The woman have be the plague spot of Lower Carmody and Carmody Harbour for a generation .

Yet Naomi have not always be an outcast .
Her girlhood have be innocent ; but she be the possessor of a dangerous beauty , and her mother be dead .

Can you help me ? ''

Sins like mine ca n't be forgive .
He ca n't -- and He wo n't . ''

That 's why I 'm skeered of him .
No , no .

Love !
There ai n't no such thing a love !

Oh , grandfather ! ''
