IV .
Little Joscelyn `` It simply be n't to be think of , Aunty Nan , '' say Mrs. William Morrison decisively .
Mrs. William Morrison be one of those people who always speak decisively .
If they merely announce that they be go to peel the potato for dinner their hearer realize that there be no possible escape for the potato .
Moreover , these people be always give their full title by everybody .
William Morrison be call Billy oftener than not ; but , if you have ask for Mrs. Billy Morrison , nobody in Avonlea would have know what you mean at first guess .
`` You must see that for yourself , Aunty , '' go on Mrs. William , hull strawberry nimbly with her large , firm , white finger a she talk .
Mrs. William always improve every shin moment .
`` It be ten mile to Kensington , and just think how late you would be get back .
You be not able for such a drive .
You would n't get over it for a month .
You know you be anything but strong this summer . ''
Aunty Nan sigh , and pat the tiny , furry , gray morsel of a kitten in her lap with tremble finger .
She know , good than anyone else could know it , that she be not strong that summer .
In her secret soul , Aunty Nan , sweet and frail and timid under the burden of her seventy year , felt with mysterious unmistakable prescience that it be to be her last summer at the Gull Point Farm .
But that be only the more reason why she should go to hear little Joscelyn sing ; she would never have another chance .
And oh , to hear little Joscelyn sing just once -- Joscelyn , whose voice be delight thousand out in the big world , just a in the year go by it have delight Aunty Nan and the dweller at the Gull Point Farm for a whole golden summer with carol at dawn and dusk about the old place !
`` Oh , I know I 'm not very strong , Maria . ''
say Aunty Nan pleadingly , `` but I be strong enough for that .
Indeed I be .
I could stay at Kensington over night with George 's folk , you know , and so it would n't tire me much .
I do so want to hear Joscelyn sing .
Oh , how I love little Joscelyn . ''
`` It pass my understanding , the way you hanker after that child , '' cry Mrs. William impatiently .
`` Why , she be a perfect stranger to you when she come here , and she be here only one summer ! ''
`` But oh , such a summer ! ''
say Aunty Nan softly .
`` We all love little Joscelyn .
She just seem like one of our own .
She be one of God 's child , carry love with them everywhere .
In some way that little Anne Shirley the Cuthberts have get up there at Green Gables remind me of her , though in other way they 're not a bit alike .
Joscelyn be a beauty . ''
`` Well , that Shirley snippet certainly be n't that , '' say Mrs. William sarcastically .
`` And if Joscelyn 's tongue be one third as long a Anne Shirley 's the wonder to me be that she do n't talk you all to death out of hand . ''
`` Little Joscelyn be n't much of a talker , '' say Aunty Nan dreamily .
`` She be kind of a quiet child .
But you remember what she do say .
And I 've never forget little Joscelyn . ''
Mrs. William shrug her plump , shapely shoulder .
`` Well , it be fifteen year ago , Aunty Nan , and Joscelyn ca n't be very ` little ' now .
She be a famous woman , and she have forget all about you , you can be sure of that . ''
`` Joscelyn be n't the kind that forget , '' say Aunty Nan loyally .
`` And , anyway , the point be , I have n't forget HER .
Oh , Maria , I 've long for year and year just to hear her sing once more .
It seem a if I MUST hear my little Joscelyn sing once again before I die .
I 've never have the chance before and I never will have it again .
Do please ask William to take me to Kensington . ''
`` Dear me , Aunty Nan , this be really childish , '' say Mrs. William , whisk her bowlful of berry into the pantry .
`` You must let other folk be the judge of what be best for you now .
You be n't strong enough to drive to Kensington , and , even if you be , you know well enough that William could n't go to Kensington to-morrow night .
He have get to attend that political meeting at Newbridge .
They ca n't do without him . ''
`` Jordan could take me to Kensington , '' plead Aunty Nan , with very unusual persistence .
`` Nonsense !
You could n't go to Kensington with the hired man .
Now , Aunty Nan , do be reasonable .
Are n't William and I kind to you ?
Do n't we do everything for your comfort ? ''
`` Yes , oh , yes , '' admit Aunty Nan deprecatingly .

I be go down to the shore field now to call William to tea .
Just keep an eye on the baby in chance he wake up , and see that the teapot do n't boil over . ''
Mrs. William whisk out of the kitchen , pretend not to see the tear that be fall over Aunty Nan 's withered pink cheek .

Why , she cry now about every little thing !
And such a notion -- to want to go to the Old Timers ' concert at Kensington and be so set on it !
Really , it be hard to put up with her whim .
Mrs. William sigh virtuously .
As for Aunty Nan , she sit alone in the kitchen , and cry bitterly , a only lonely old age can cry .
It seem to her that she could not bear it , that she MUST go to Kensington .
But she know that it be not to be , since Mrs. William have decide otherwise .
Mrs. William 's word be law at Gull Point Farm .
`` What 's the matter with my old Aunty Nan ? ''

Jordan Sloane stand there , his round , freckle face look a anxious and sympathetic a it be possible for such a very round , very freckled face to look .

No , I 'm not , Jordan , I know it .
Something tell me so very plainly .

ask Jordan .

`` No , but dearer to me -- dearer to me than many of my own .

Even Maria herself would n't , if she have know her .
It be fifteen year since she come here one summer to board .
She be a child of thirteen then , and have n't any relation except an old uncle who send her to school in winter and board her out in summer , and do n't care a rap about her .
The child be just starve for love , Jordan , and she get it here .
William and his brother be just child then , and they have n't any sister .
We all just worship her .
She be so sweet , Jordan .
And pretty , oh my !
like a little girl in a picture , with great long curl , all black and purply and fine a spun silk , and big dark eye , and such pink cheek -- real wild rise cheek .
And sing !
My land !

Always sing , every hour of the day that voice be ring round the old place .

It be bear in her .
Sunday evening she use to sing hymn for u .

` Twould n't be any wonder .

Only sixteen mile away -- and I ca n't go . ''

Jordan detest Mrs. William , but she be a power to be reckon with , all the same .
Meek , easy-going Billy Morrison do just what his wife tell him to .

Need n't mention it to headquarters . ''
Billy Morrison 's face be solemn , but Jordan wink a he pocket the money .

Billy Morrison have agree to pasture the animal if Jordan use him in the farm work , an arrangement scoffed at by Mrs. William in no measured term .

`` Joscelyn Burnett , the famous contralto , be spend a few day in Kensington on her return from her Maritime concert tour .

March right up now .
Think of Aunty Nan and do n't let style down you . ''

Jordan 's round , freckle countenance grow crimson .

What could he say to her ?
How could he say it ?

she say .

Is she well ?

It be sunset when they reach Gull Point Farm .
An arc of warm gold be over the spruce behind the house .

`` I 'll take you right up to her room . ''

Little Joscelyn ! ''
in a tone that make him choke again .

She 's up with her now . ''

You have n't change a bit .
And you 're a famous singer now , little Joscelyn !
I always know you would be .
Oh , I want you to sing a piece for me -- just one , wo n't you , dearie ?
Sing that piece people like to hear you sing best .
I forget the name , but I 've read about it in the paper .
Sing it for me , little Joscelyn . ''
And Joscelyn , stand by Aunty Nan 's bed , in the sunset light , sing the song she have sing to many a brilliant audience on many a noted concert-platform -- sing it a even she have never sing before , while Aunty Nan lay and listen beatifically , and downstairs even Mrs. William hold her breath , entrance by the exquisite melody that float through the old farmhouse .
`` O , little Joscelyn ! ''
breathe Aunty Nan in rapture , when the song end .

One by one they recall the memory of that vanished summer .
The past give up it tear and it laughter .
Heart and fancy alike go roam through the way of the long ago .
Aunty Nan be perfectly happy .

I ai n't never forget how you use to sing that , and I want to hear it just once again , dearie .
Sing it for me , little Joscelyn . ''
Joscelyn rise and go to the window .

At first Aunty Nan beat time to it feebly on the counterpane ; but when Joscelyn come to the verse , `` With mercy and with judgment , '' she fold her hand over her breast and smile .
When the hymn end , Joscelyn come over to the bed .
`` I be afraid I must say good-bye now , Aunty Nan , '' she say .

No , thank you , I do n't care for any refreshment .

I come out to see Aunty Nan . ''

`` Fancy now , '' say Mrs. William , rather overcome at hear this great singer pronounce such an encomium on quiet , timid old Aunty Nan .

Thus it be that Mrs. William find her , go in the next morning with her breakfast .
The sunlight crept over the pillow , light up the sweet old face and silver hair , and steal downward to the fade red rose on her breast .
Smiling and peaceful and happy lay Aunty Nan , for she have fall on the sleep that know no earthy wakening , while little Joscelyn sang .
V .
The Winning of Lucinda The marriage of a Penhallow be always the signal for a gathering of the Penhallows .
From the uttermost part of the earth they would come -- Penhallows by birth , and Penhallows by marriage and Penhallows by ancestry .
East Grafton be the ancient habitat of the race , and Penhallow Grange , where `` old '' John Penhallow live , be a Mecca to them .
As for the family itself , the exact kinship of all it various branch and ramification be a hard thing to define .
Old Uncle Julius Penhallow be look upon a a veritable wonder because he carry it all in his head and could tell on sight just what relation any one Penhallow be to any other Penhallow .
The rest make a blind guess at it , for the most part , and the young Penhallows let it go at loose cousinship .
In this instance it be Alice Penhallow , daughter of `` young '' John Penhallow , who be to be marry .
Alice be a nice girl , but she and her wed only pertain to this story in so far a they furnish a background for Lucinda ; hence nothing more need be say of her .
On the afternoon of her wedding day -- the Penhallows hold to the good , old-fashioned custom of even wedding with a rouse dance afterwards -- Penhallow Grange be fill to overflow with guest who have come there to have tea and rest themselves before go down to `` young '' John 's .
Many of them have drive fifty mile .
In the big autumnal orchard the young fry foregather and chat and coquet .
Up-stairs , in `` old '' Mrs. John 's bedroom , she and her married daughter hold high conclave .
`` Old '' John have establish himself with his son and son-in-law in the parlour , and the three daughter-in-law be make themselves at home in the blue sitting-room , ear-deep in harmless family gossip .
Lucinda and Romney Penhallow be also there .
Thin Mrs. Nathaniel Penhallow sit in a rocking chair and toast her toe at the grate , for the brilliant autumn afternoon be slightly chilly and Lucinda , a usual , have the window open .
She and plump Mrs. Frederick Penhallow do most of the talk .
Mrs. George Penhallow be rather out of it by reason of her newness .
She be George Penhallow 's second wife , marry only a year .
Hence , her contribution to the conversation be rather spasmodic , hurl in , a it be , by dead reckoning , be sometimes appropriate and sometimes savouring of a point of view not strictly Penhallowesque .
Romney Penhallow be sit in a corner , listen to the chatter of the woman , with the inscrutable smile that always vex Mrs. Frederick .
Mrs. George wonder within herself what he do there among the woman .
She also wonder just where he belong on the family tree .
He be not one of the uncle , yet he could not be much young than George .
`` Forty , if he be a day , '' be Mrs. George 's mental dictum , `` but a very handsome and fascinating man .
I never saw such a splendid chin and dimple . ''
Lucinda , with bronze-colored hair and the white of skin , defiant of merciless sunlight and revelling in the crisp air , sit on the sill of the open window behind the crimson vine leaf , look out into the garden , where dahlia flame and aster break into wave of purple and snow .
The ruddy light of the autumn afternoon give a sheen to the wave of her hair and bring out the exceed purity of her Greek outline .
Mrs. George know who Lucinda be -- a cousin of the second generation , and , in spite of her thirty-five year , the acknowledged beauty of the whole Penhallow connection .
She be one of those rare woman who keep their loveliness unmarred by the passage of year .
She have ripen and mature , but she have not grow old .
The old Penhallows be still incline , from sheer force of habit , to look upon her a a girl , and the young Penhallows hail her a one of themselves .
Yet Lucinda never ape girlishness ; good taste and a strong sense of humour preserve her amid many temptation thereto .
She be simply a beautiful , fully develop woman , with whom Time have declare a truce , young with a mellow youth which have nothing to do with year .
Mrs. George like and admire Lucinda .
Now , when Mrs. George like and admire any person , it be a matter of necessity with her to impart her opinion to the most convenient confidant .
In this case it be Romney Penhallow to whom Mrs. George remark sweetly : `` Really , do n't you think our Lucinda be look remarkably well this fall ? ''
It seem a very harmless , inane , well-meant question .
Poor Mrs. George might well be excuse for feeling bewilder over the effect .
Romney gather his long leg together , stand up , and sweep the unfortunate speaker a crush Penhallow bow of state .
`` Far be it from me to disagree with the opinion of a lady -- especially when it concern another lady , '' he say , a he leave the blue room .
Overcome by the mordant satire in his tone , Mrs. George glance speechlessly at Lucinda .
Behold , Lucinda have squarely turn her back on the party and be gaze out into the garden , with a very decided flush on the snowy curve of her neck and cheek .
Then Mrs. George look at her sister-in-law .
They be regard her with the tolerant amusement they might bestow on a blundering child .
Mrs. George experience that subtle prescience whereby it be give u to know that we have put our foot in it .
She felt herself turn an uncomfortable brick-red .
What Penhallow skeleton have she unwittingly jangle ?
Why , oh , why , be it such an evident breach of the propriety to praise Lucinda ?
Mrs. George be devoutly thankful that a summons to the tea-table rescue her from her mire of embarrassment .
The meal be spoil for her , however ; the mortify recollection of her mysterious blunder conspire with her curiosity to banish appetite .
As soon a possible after tea she decoy Mrs. Frederick out into the garden and in the dahlia walk solemnly demand the reason of it all .
Mrs. Frederick indulge in a laugh which put the mettle of her festal brown silk seam to the test .
`` My dear Cecilia , it be SO amusing , '' she say , a little patronizingly .
`` But WHY ! ''
cried Mrs. George , resent the patronage and the mystery .
`` What be so dreadful in what I say ?
Or so funny ?
And WHO be this Romney Penhallow who must n't be speak to ? ''
`` Oh , Romney be one of the Charlottetown Penhallows , '' explain Mrs. Frederick .
`` He be a lawyer there .
He be a first cousin of Lucinda 's and a second of George 's -- or be he ?
Oh , bother !
You must go to Uncle John if you want the genealogy .
I 'm in a chronic muddle concern Penhallow relationship .
And , a for Romney , of course you can speak to him about anything you like except Lucinda .
Oh , you innocent !
To ask him if he do n't think Lucinda be look well !
And right before her , too !
Of course he think you do it on purpose to tease him .
That be what make him so savage and sarcastic . ''
`` But WHY ? ''
persist Mrs. George , stick tenaciously to her point .
`` Has n't George tell you ? ''
`` No , '' say George 's wife in mild exasperation .
`` George have spend most of his time since we be marry tell me odd thing about the Penhallows , but he have n't get to that yet , evidently . ''
`` Why , my dear , it be our family romance .
Lucinda and Romney be in love with each other .
They have be in love with each other for fifteen year and in all that time they have never speak to each other once ! ''
`` Dear me ! ''
murmur Mrs. George , feel the inadequacy of mere language .
Was this a Penhallow method of courtship ?
`` But WHY ? ''
`` They have a quarrel fifteen year ago , '' say Mrs. Frederick patiently .
`` Nobody know how it originate or anything about it except that Lucinda herself admit it to u afterwards .
But , in the first flush of her rage , she tell Romney that she would never speak to him again as long a she live .
And HE say he would never speak to her until she speak first -- because , you see , a she be in the wrong she ought to make the first advance .
And they never have speak .
Everybody in the connection , I suppose , have take turn try to reconcile them , but nobody have succeed .
I do n't believe that Romney have ever so much a THOUGHT of any other woman in his whole life , and certainly Lucinda have never think of any other man .
You will notice she still wear Romney 's ring .
They 're practically engage still , of course .
And Romney say once that if Lucinda would just say one word , no matter what it be , even if it be something insulting , he would speak , too , and beg her pardon for his share in the quarrel -- because then , you see , he would not be break his word .
He have n't refer to the matter for year , but I presume that he be of the same mind still .
And they be just as much in love with each other a they ever be .
He 's always hang about where she be -- when other people be there , too , that be .
He avoid her like a plague when she be alone .
That be why he be stick out in the blue room with u to-day .
There do n't seem to be a particle of resentment between them .
If Lucinda would only speak !
But that Lucinda will not do . ''
`` Do n't you think she will yet ? ''
say Mrs. George .
Mrs. Frederick shake her crimped head sagely .
`` Not now .
The whole thing have harden too long .
Her pride will never let her speak .
We use to hope she would be trick into it by forgetfulness or accident -- we use to lay trap for her -- but all to no effect .
It be such a shame , too .
They be make for each other .
Do you know , I get cross when I begin to thrash the whole silly affair over like this .
Does n't it sound a if we be talk of the quarrel of two school-children ?
Of late year we have learn that it do not do to speak of Lucinda to Romney , even in the most commonplace way .
He seem to resent it . ''
`` HE ought to speak , '' cry Mrs. George warmly .
`` Even if she be in the wrong ten time over , he ought to overlook it and speak first . ''
`` But he wo n't .
And she wo n't .
You never saw two such determine mortal .
They get it from their grandfather on the mother 's side -- old Absalom Gordon .
There be no such stubbornness on the Penhallow side .
His obstinacy be a proverb , my dear -- actually a proverb .
What ever he say , he would stick to if the sky fell .
He be a terrible old man to swear , too , '' add Mrs. Frederick , drop into irrelevant reminiscence .
`` He spend a long while in a mining camp in his young day and he never get over it -- the habit of swearing , I mean .
It would have make your blood run cold , my dear , to have heard him go on at time .
And yet he be a real good old man every other way .
He could n't help it someway .
He try to , but he use to say that profanity come a natural to him a breathing .
It use to mortify his family terribly .
Fortunately , none of them take after him in that respect .
But he 's dead -- and one should n't speak ill of the dead .
I must go and get Mattie Penhallow to do my hair .
I would burst these sleeve clean out if I try to do it myself and I do n't want to dress over again .
You wo n't be likely to talk to Romney about Lucinda again , my dear Cecilia ? ''
`` Fifteen year ! ''
murmur Mrs. George helplessly to the dahlia .
`` Engaged for fifteen year and never speak to each other !
Dear heart and soul , think of it !
Oh , these Penhallows ! ''
Meanwhile , Lucinda , serenely unconscious that her love story be be mouth over by Mrs. Frederick in the dahlia garden , be dress for the wedding .

Moreover , she have a new dress .
Now , a new dress -- and especially one as nice a this -- be a rarity with Lucinda , who belong to a branch of the Penhallows note for be chronically hard up .
Indeed , Lucinda and her widowed mother be positively poor , and hence a new dress be an event in Lucinda 's existence .
An uncle have give her this one -- a beautiful , perishable thing , such a Lucinda would never have dare to choose for herself , but in which she revel with feminine delight .
It be of pale green voile -- a colour which bring out admirably the ruddy gloss of her hair and the clear brilliance of her skin .

Lucinda be not vain , but she be quite well aware of the fact of her beauty and take an impersonal pleasure in it , a if she be look at some finely paint picture by a master hand .
The form and face reflect in the glass satisfy her .
The puff and drapery of the green voile display to perfection the full , but not over-full , curve of her fine figure .
Lucinda lift her arm and touch a red rise to her lip with the hand upon which shine the frosty glitter of Romney 's diamond , look at the graceful slope of her shoulder and the splendid line of chin and throat with critical approval .
She note , too , how well the gown become her eye , bring out all the deep colour in them .
Lucinda have magnificent eye .
Once Romney have write a sonnet to them in which he compare their colour to ripe blueberry .
This may not sound poetical to you unless you know or remember just what the tint of ripe blueberry be -- dusky purple in some light , clear slate in others , and yet again in others the misty hue of early meadow violet .

That be what it mean when social function begin to bore you . ''

No , she would walk , since that be all there be to it ; but she would not go by the main road to be star at by all and sundry who might pass her .

Lucinda recognize him with a great deal of annoyance and a little relief .
She would not have to walk home alone .
But with Romney Penhallow !
Would he think she have contrive it so purposely ?
Romney silently open the gate for her , silently latch it behind her , and silently fell into step beside her .
Down across a velvety sweep of field they go ; the air be frosty , calm and still ; over the world lay a haze of moonshine and mist that convert East Grafton 's prosaic hill and field into a shimmering fairyland .
At first Lucinda felt angrier than ever .
What a ridiculous situation !
How the Penhallows would laugh over it !
As for Romney , he , too , be angry with the trick impish chance have play him .
He like be the butt of an awkward situation as little a most men ; and certainly to be oblige to walk home over moonlit field at one o'clock in the morning with the woman he have love and never speak to for fifteen year be the irony of fate with a vengeance .
Would she think he have scheme for it ?
And how the deuce do she come to be walk home from the wedding at all ?
By the time they have cross the field and reach the wild cherry lane beyond it , Lucinda 's anger be master by her save sense of humour .
She be even smile a little maliciously under her fascinator .

The moonshine fell through the arching bough and make a mosaic of silver light and clear-cut shadow for the unfriendly lover to walk in .
On either side be the hovering gloom of the wood , and around them be a great silence unstirred by wind or murmur .

She think of the last time Romney and she have walk home together through this very lane , from a party at `` young '' John 's .
It have be moonlight then too , and -- Lucinda check a sigh -- they have walk hand in hand .

Past the lane a range of three silvery harvest field slop down to Peter Penhallow 's brook -- a wide , shallow stream bridge over in the olden day by the mossy trunk of an ancient fallen tree .
When Lucinda and Romney arrive at the brook they gaze at the brawling water blankly .

There be no tree !
There be no bridge of any kind over the brook !
Here be a predicament !

She could not forbid him and she be so choked with rage over his presumption that she could not have speak in any case .

Romney 's foot slip on a treacherous round stone -- there be a tremendous splash -- and Romney and Lucinda Penhallow be sit down in the middle of Peter Penhallow 's brook .
Lucinda be the first to regain her foot .

`` YOU D -- D IDIOT ! ''
she say , in a voice that literally shake with rage .

`` I 'm awfully sorry , Lucinda , '' he say , strive with uncertain success to keep a suspicious quiver of laughter out of his tone .

Then Lucinda begin to shake with inward laughter .
She laugh silently for the whole length of the field ; and at the line fence between Peter Penhallow 's land and the Grange acres she pause , throw back the fascinator from her face , and look at Romney defiantly .
`` You be think of -- THAT , '' she cry , `` and I be think of it .
And we will go on , think of it at interval for the rest of our life .

There be more than a suspicion of laughter in his voice this time , but Lucinda do not choose to resent it .
She do not speak again until they reach the Grange gate .
Then she face him solemnly .
`` It be a case of atavism , '' she say .
`` Old Grandfather Gordon be to blame for it . ''
At the Grange almost everybody be in bed .
What with the guest straggle home at interval and hurry sleepily off to their room , nobody have miss Lucinda , each set suppose she be with some other set .

`` Lucinda Penhallow ! ''
gasped they , one and all .
`` I be leave to walk home , '' say Lucinda coolly .
`` So Romney and I come across the field .
There be no bridge over the brook , and when he be carry me over he slip and we fell in .
That be all .
No , Cecilia , I never take cold , so do n't worry .
Yes , my dress be ruin , but that be of no consequence .

Good night . ''

`` Oh , WHAT do you suppose she say to him ? ''
cried Mrs. George .

They never do know .
VI .
Old Man Shaw 's Girl `` Day after to-morrow -- day after to-morrow , '' say Old Man Shaw , rub his long slender hand together gleefully .
`` I have to keep say it over and over , so a to really believe it .
It seem far too good to be true that I 'm to have Blossom again .
And everything be ready .
Yes , I think everything be ready , except a bit of cooking .
And wo n't this orchard be a surprise to her !
I 'm just go to bring her out here as soon a I can , never say a word .
I 'll fetch her through the spruce lane , and when we come to the end of the path I 'll step back casual-like , and let her go out from under the tree alone , never suspect .
It 'll be worth ten time the trouble to see her big , brown eye open wide and hear her say , ` Oh , daddy !
Why , daddy ! ' ''
He rub his hand again and laugh softly to himself .
He be a tall , bent old man , whose hair be snow white , but whose face be fresh and rosy .
His eye be a boy 's eye , large , blue and merry , and his mouth have never get over a youthful trick of smile at any provocation -- and , oft-times , at no provocation at all .
To be sure , White Sands people would not have give you the most favourable opinion in the world of Old Man Shaw .
First and foremost , they would have tell you that he be `` shiftless , '' and have let his bit of a farm run out while he potter with flower and bug , or ramble aimlessly about in the wood , or read book along the shore .
Perhaps it be true ; but the old farm yield him a living , and far than that Old Man Shaw have no ambition .
He be as blithe a a pilgrim on a pathway climb to the west .
He have learn the rare secret that you must take happiness when you find it -- that there be no use in mark the place and come back to it at a more convenient season , because it will not be there then .
And it be very easy to be happy if you know , a Old Man Shaw most thoroughly knew , how to find pleasure in little thing .
He enjoy life , he have always enjoy life and help others to enjoy it ; consequently his life be a success , whatever White Sands people might think of it .
What if he have not `` improve '' his farm ?
There be some people to whom life will never be anything more than a kitchen garden ; and there be others to whom it will always be a royal palace with dome and minaret of rainbow fancy .
The orchard of which he be so proud be a yet little more than the substance of thing hop for -- a flourishing plantation of young tree which would amount to something later on .
Old Man Shaw 's house be on the crest of a bare , sunny hill , with a few staunch old fir and spruce behind it -- the only tree that could resist the full sweep of the wind that blow bitterly up from the sea at time .
Fruit tree would never grow near it , and this have be a great grief to Sara .
`` Oh , daddy , if we could just have an orchard ! ''
she have be wont to say wistfully , when other farmhouse in White Sands be smother whitely in apple bloom .
And when she have go away , and her father have nothing to look forward to save her return , he be determine she should find an orchard when she come back .
Over the southward hill , warmly shelter by spruce wood and slop to the sunshine , be a little field , so fertile that all the slack management of a life-time have not avail to exhaust it .
Here Old Man Shaw set out his orchard and saw it flourish , watch and tend it until he come to know each tree a a child and love it .
His neighbour laugh at him , and say that the fruit of an orchard so far away from the house would all be steal .
But a yet there be no fruit , and when the time come for bearing there would be enough and to spare .
`` Blossom and me 'll get all we want , and the boy can have the rest , if they want 'em bad 'n they want a good conscience , '' say that unworldly , unbusinesslike Old Man Shaw .
On his way back home from his darling orchard he find a rare fern in the wood and dug it up for Sara -- she have love fern .
He plant it at the shady , shelter side of the house and then sit down on the old bench by the garden gate to read her last letter -- the letter that be only a note , because she be come home soon .
He know every word of it by heart , but that do not spoil the pleasure of re-reading it every half-hour .
Old Man Shaw have not marry until late in life , and have , so White Sands people say , select a wife with his usual judgment -- which , be interpret , mean no judgment at all ; otherwise , he would never have marry Sara Glover , a mere slip of a girl , with big brown eye like a frightened wood creature 's , and the delicate , fleet bloom of a spring Mayflower .
`` The last woman in the world for a farmer 's wife -- no strength or get-up about her . ''
Neither could White Sands folk understand what on earth Sara Glover have marry him for .
`` Well , the fool crop be the only one that never fail . ''
Old Man Shaw -- he be Old Man Shaw even then , although he be only forty -- and his girl bride have trouble themselves not at all about White Sands opinion .
They have one year of perfect happiness , which be always worth living for , even if the rest of life be a dreary pilgrimage , and then Old Man Shaw find himself alone again , except for little Blossom .
She be christen Sara , after her dead mother , but she be always Blossom to her father -- the precious little blossom whose plucking have cost the mother her life .
Sara Glover 's people , especially a wealthy aunt in Montreal , have want to take the child , but Old Man Shaw grow almost fierce over the suggestion .
He would give his baby to no one .
A woman be hire to look after the house , but it be the father who care for the baby in the main .
He be a tender and faithful and deft a a woman .
Sara never miss a mother 's care , and she grow up into a creature of life and light and beauty , a constant delight to all who know her .
She have a way of embroider life with star .
She be dower with all the charm characteristic of both parent , with a resilient vitality and activity which have pertain to neither of them .
When she be ten year old she have pack all hireling off , and keep house for her father for six delightful year -- year in which they be father and daughter , brother and sister , and `` chum . ''
Sara never go to school , but her father saw to her education after a fashion of his own .
When their work be do they live in the wood and field , in the little garden they have make on the sheltered side of the house , or on the shore , where sunshine and storm be to them equally lovely and beloved .
Never be comradeship more perfect or more wholly satisfactory .
`` Just wrap up in each other , '' say White Sands folk , half-enviously , half-disapprovingly .
When Sara be sixteen Mrs. Adair , the wealthy aunt aforesaid , pounce down on White Sands in a glamour of fashion and culture and outer worldliness .
She bombard Old Man Shaw with such argument that he have to succumb .
It be a shame that a girl like Sara should grow up in a place like White Sands , `` with no advantage and no education , '' say Mrs. Adair scornfully , not understand that wisdom and knowledge be two entirely different thing .
`` At least let me give my dear sister 's child what I would have give my own daughter if I have have one , '' she plead tearfully .
`` Let me take her with me and send her to a good school for a few year .
Then , if she wish , she may come back to you , of course . ''
Privately , Mrs. Adair do not for a moment believe that Sara would want to come back to White Sands , and her queer old father , after three year of the life she would give her .
Old Man Shaw yield , influence thereto not at all by Mrs. Adair 's readily flow tear , but greatly by his conviction that justice to Sara demand it .
Sara herself do not want to go ; she protest and plead ; but her father , have become convinced that it be best for her to go , be inexorable .
Everything , even her own feeling , must give way to that .
But she be to come back to him without let or hindrance when her `` schooling '' be do .
It be only on have this most clearly understood that Sara would consent to go at all .
Her last word , call back to her father through her tear a she and her aunt drive down the lane , be , `` I 'll be back , daddy .
In three year I 'll be back .
Do n't cry , but just look forward to that . ''
He have look forward to it through the three long , lonely year that follow , in all of which he never saw his darling .
Half a continent be between them and Mrs. Adair have veto vacation visit , under some specious pretense .
But every week bring it letter from Sara .
Old Man Shaw have every one of them , tie up with one of her old blue hair ribbon , and keep in her mother 's little rose-wood work-box in the parlour .
He spend every Sunday afternoon re-reading them , with her photograph before him .
He live alone , refuse to be pester with kind help , but he keep the house in beautiful order .
`` A good housekeeper than farmer , '' say White Sands people .
He would have nothing alter .
When Sara come back she be not to be hurt by change .
It never occur to him that she might be change herself .
And now those three interminable year be go , and Sara be come home .
She write him nothing of her aunt 's pleading and reproach and ready , futile tear ; she write only that she would graduate in June and start for home a week later .
Thenceforth Old Man Shaw go about in a state of beatitude , make ready for her homecoming .
As he sit on the bench in the sunshine , with the blue sea sparkling and crinkle down at the foot of the green slope , he reflect with satisfaction that all be in perfect order .
There be nothing left to do save count the hour until that beautiful , longed-for day after to-morrow .
He give himself over to a reverie , as sweet a a day-dream in a haunted valley .
The red rose be out in bloom .
Sara have always love those red rose -- they be as vivid a herself , with all her own fullness of life and joy of living .
And , besides these , a miracle have happen in Old Man Shaw 's garden .
In one corner be a rose-bush which have never bloom , despite all the coax they have give it -- `` the sulky rose-bush , '' Sara have be wont to call it .
Lo !
this summer have fling the hoarded sweetness of year into plentiful white blossom , like shallow ivory cup with a haunting , spicy fragrance .
It be in honour of Sara 's home-coming -- so Old Man Shaw like to fancy .
All thing , even the sulky rose-bush , know she be come back , and be make glad because of it .
He be gloat over Sara 's letter when Mrs. Peter Blewett come .
She tell him she have run up to see how he be get on , and if he want anything see to before Sara come .
`` No 'm , thank you , ma'am .
Everything be attend to .
I could n't let anyone else prepare for Blossom .
Only to think , ma'am , she 'll be home the day after to-morrow .
I 'm just fill clear through , body , soul , and spirit , with joy to think of have my little Blossom at home again . ''

When Mrs. Blewett smile it foretokened trouble , and wise people have learn to have sudden business elsewhere before the smile could be translate into word .
But Old Man Shaw have never learn to be wise where Mrs. Blewett be concern , although she have be his near neighbour for year , and have pester his life out with advice and `` neighbourly turn . ''
Mrs. Blewett be one with whom life have go awry .
The effect on her be to render happiness to other people a personal insult .
She resent Old Man Shaw 's beam delight in his daughter 's return , and she `` consider it her duty '' to rub the bloom off straightway .
`` Do you think Sary 'll be content in White Sands now ? ''
she ask .
Old Man Shaw look slightly bewilder .
`` Of course she 'll be content , '' he say slowly .
`` Is n't it her home ?
And ai n't I here ? ''
Mrs. Blewett smile again , with double distilled contempt for such simplicity .
`` Well , it 's a good thing you 're so sure of it , I suppose .
If 't be my daughter that be come back to White Sands , after three year of fashionable life among rich , stylish folk , and at a swell school , I would n't have a minute 's peace of mind .
I 'd know perfectly well that she 'd look down on everything here , and be discontent and miserable . ''

Mrs. Blewett shrug her sharp shoulder .
`` Maybe not .
It 's to be hop not , for both your sake , I 'm sure .
But I 'd be worry if 't be me .
Sary 's be live among fine folk , and have a gay , exciting time , and it stand to reason she 'll think White Sands fearful lonesome and dull .
Look at Lauretta Bradley .
She be up in Boston for just a month last winter and she 's never be able to endure White Sands since . ''
`` Lauretta Bradley and Sara Shaw be two different people , '' say Sara 's father , try to smile .

`` It 's such a queer , little , old place .
What 'll she think of it after her aunt 's ?
I 've hear tell Mrs. Adair live in a perfect palace .
I 'll just warn you kindly that Sary 'll probably look down on you , and you might as well be prepare for it .
Of course , I suppose she kind of think she have to come back , see she promise you so solemn she would .
But I 'm certain she do n't want to , and I do n't blame her either . ''
Even Mrs. Blewett have to stop for breath , and Old Man Shaw find his opportunity .
He have listen , daze and shrink , a if she be deal him physical blow , but now a swift change sweep over him .
His blue eye flash ominously , straight into Mrs. Blewett 's straggling , ferrety gray orb .
`` If you 're say your say , Martha Blewett , you can go , '' he say passionately .
`` I 'm not go to listen to another such word .
Take yourself out of my sight , and your malicious tongue out of my hearing ! ''

His delight be dead ; his heart be full of pain and bitterness .
Martha Blewett be a warped and ill-natured woman , but he fear there be altogether too much truth in what she say .
Why have he never think of it before ?
Of course White Sands would seem dull and lonely to Blossom ; of course the little gray house where she be bear would seem a poor abode after the splendour of her aunt 's home .

How poor and simple everything be !
How sagging and weather-beaten the old house !
He go in , and up-stairs to Sara 's room .
It be neat and clean , just a she have leave it three year ago .
But it be small and dark ; the ceiling be discolour , the furniture old-fashioned and shabby ; she would think it a poor , mean place .
Even the orchard over the hill bring him no comfort now .
Blossom would not care for orchard .
She would be ashamed of her stupid old father and the barren farm .
She would hate White Sands , and chafe at the dull existence , and look down on everything that go to make up his uneventful life .

He saw himself a he think White Sands folk must see him -- a poor , shiftless , foolish old man , who have only one thing in the world worthwhile , his little girl , and have not be of enough account to keep her .
